“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tindive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Ausuiaiia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System-one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency,
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
or.a has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now aiflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the peonle over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected, with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that' so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maonland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently reraailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

A FATAL FASCINATION.
"On Sunday afternoon Cardinal Moran referred to the wave of depression which, he said, they all regretted had swept over their own and the neighbouring provinces.
There were lessons which their present experience tanght and which he trusted would be taken to heart by the Government and by the people. This vice of gambling had
spread like some dread epidemic throughout the length and land, and he would rejoice to see some crusade started winch would have the effect of checking its
growth and finally rooting it out altogether."

SATURDAY, JUNE 18, 1892.
Let us Kill Somebody.
In an issue of St. James’ Gazette, which
reached us by last mail, there appears an
interesting letter from that accustomed cor-
respondent who rises at intervals to ask why
Australia should not take a hand in the
military service of the Empire, and share
the glories of the tented field with the full-
blown private of Britain. There is a sad,
depressing sameness about that corres-
pondent, inasmuch as he always assumes in
a certain irresponsible manner that is all his
own—(1) That Australia has no voice in the
matter, and that it is only necessary for the
British war department to decide that
certain troops ought to be “raised” in this
country, and they shall be raised accordingly;
and (2) that Australia doesn’t want any
voice in the matter, but would rush wildly
to seize the opportunity by the forelock. “It
is a fine day,” says the military Briton, in
effect ; “ let us go out and kill somebody
and then in a sudden accession of cordiality
he offers to take the Australian out with
him so that the latter can kill somebody too.
The idea that the offer might be refused—
that the Australian might not want to kill
anybody at present, except perhaps his own
aborigine—never enters into the calculation.
In this respect the warlike Briton very much
resembles the courteous cannibal gentleman
with nothing on, who offers his visitor the
first drink of blood out of the butchered
preacher—neither ever considers the pos-
sibility that the blood might not be
appreciated.
* * *
The British army, as pointed out by the
correspondent of St. James Gazette, suffers
from a perennial scarcity of recruits. The
pay is so utterly wretched, the victuals so
infamous, the prospects of promotion are so
scarce and the prospects of dying in the
workhouse in later life so plentiful, the
insults from ignorant and asinine officers are
so regular, and the swindle is so reliable -
for the British Government hires its red-
coated hero at a wage of Is. per day and
“everything found," and then it charges
him for his clothes, medical attendance,
hair-cutting, use of library, damages to
barracks, a great part of his food, and other
sundries till his alleged pay is almost entirely
vanished - that soldiers arc practically un-
procurable. Out of a nation of 38,000,000
warlike souls an army 210.000 strong is
maintained with the utmost difficulty. The
majority of the recruits consist of stunted
youths under 20 years of age—youths of
the gin and short-pipe variety who have a
father in gaol, and an uncle in the lunatic
asylum, and a mother who is an incurable
dipsomaniac—and of these 4000 desert
annually, and over 10,000 are court-mar-
tialled. Therefore the British army is in a
bad way, especially that part of it which is
stationed in the unhealthy regions of India,
and the addition of a few new battalions is
urgently wanted. Australia is to supply
these battalions, and how she is to do it is as
follows :
I venture to submit for consideration the desirability
of raising three battalions of infantry in Australia for ser-
' ice in India. I believe that the more populous colonies
could easily furnish the men for them, and that
it would be greatly to their advantage to do so. To put
the matter as clearly as possible, £ will summarise the
advantages which I believe that the adoption of this pro-
posal would carry, (a) Three battalions of British in-
fantry could be withdrawn from India, thus restoring the
balance to a great extent ;(6) India would be strengthened
b\ three battalions of men already inured to a hot
climate ; (c) the Australian colonies would in a short time
possess a reserve of men who have received the best
possible military training ; (rf) a close connection between
Australia and India would be established ; (<■) the great
chain of mutual support and assistance which should exist
between the mother country and the colonies would be
strengthened and developed ; (/) lastly, a considerable
relief would be afforded to the annual task of the
Inspector-General of Recruiting. In answer to a possible
objection, it might be well to state that the three Austra-
lian battalions are only proposed to meet part of the
double battalion difficulty. The preponderance in num-
ber of those abroad is six, and not three. It is, how-
ever, hoped that in time the additional battalions now
required in Egypt may be withdrawn, and then
India alone would upset the balance. Consequently the
Austiahan battalions would restore it as far as need be.
The officering and recruiting of these battalions would, it
is believed be easy. Australia has its labour question and
* ' lnen ?P‘dyed, even as England has ; and the experience
of .Suakim leads us to think that there is a strong military
spirit in Englishmen of the colonies as well as in those at
home. The conditions of service, rates of pay, &c,,
shnidd be precisely the same in the Australian as in the
British infantry battalions. The senior officers would, at
toe first, have to be selected carefully from the British
army ; but all the subalterns should be Australian gentle-
**2? n . > am l after a few years there would be no more
efficient and genuinely territorial regiments than those
furnished by Australia,
* * *
As regards the advantages set forth in the
above list the majority can be dismissed in a
sentence. Ihe fact that three battalions of
British troops can be withdrawn from India,
provided an Australian force will take their
place, is of no more importance to us than
the fact that three battalions of Chinese
might be withdrawn from Corea under the
some conditions. The circumstance that
India would be “strengthened ” under these
circumstances is probable, but eminently un-
interesting-—Gehenna would be strengthened,
for that matter, if the three battalions wont
the) e, but that also is not a thrilling
consideration. After this comes the state-
ment that “a close connection between
Australia and India would be established,”
but no one wants that connection, and it we
had it, it is neither eatable nor drinkable
nor capable of being put up the spout, and
beyond the fact that the connection might
lead to cholera, and that it certainly
would lead to hobnail liver and parch-
ment kidney, there are no apparent results
in sight anywhere. The remark about the
chain of mutual support and assist-
ance ” is eloquent, but unsatisfactory and
meaningless, and the “annual task of the
Inspector-General of Recruiting” is dis-
tinctly “not our funeral.” All these
things may be reasons why England should
want troops, but they arc no earthly
reason why Australia should supply them.
The fact that England wants soldiers is in
itself by no means surprising. Every country
wants something, and most of them want
everything, and nearly every separate in-
habitant in each country wants the earth.
* * #
In addition to all these inducements, more-
over, the Australians are to enjoy “ the same
conditions of service, rates of pay,” etc., as
the ordinary British infantry-man now
wallows in. This is about as great an in-
ducement as if we were assured that the
Australian trooper would be disembowelled
and then guillotined. 1 hat warrior, in the
Soudan Contingent days, got 355. per week
as his minimum wage, and travelled in a
first-class steamer, and was victualled after
the fashion that prevails in an expensive
hotel. He carried wine and potted tongue
and bloater-paste and jelly along with him
in huge quantities—there was so much wine,
in fact, that even the dry Contingent couldn’t
finish it, and the last of it was only got rid
of the other day, when it was despatched to
the big New South Walesßenevolent Asylum.
And now this same individual is offered—as
a special privilege—the chance of enlisting
at Is. per diem, and being defrauded out of
eightpence, of wearing a glaring red coat,
and a hard, prickly shirt, and misfit boots,
of meeting with cholera and cobras, and
getting a hideous liver complaint, of being
court-martialled, black-holed, subjected to
punishment-drill, and bossed by a variety of
pompous officers of all degrees, and of having
the blood of various niggers upon his soul—
if he has a soul, and going to Sheol-jf there
is a Sheol—when he dies, in consequence.
And, further, the subalterns of the new force
are to be “exclusively Australian gentle-
men the sons of the Old Colonial Gentry*,
man, all of the present time ; and they will
return alter their term of service to be
incipient colonels in their native land, and
will be alluded to in the papers as “ military
authorities,” and will profess to regard
everything from a “ military standpoint,”
and clamour after fortifications and coinpul-
smy military service, and howl fur blood and
big guns whenever a shearers’ strike wants
suppressing, or a political demonstration
offends their sense of “ loyalty,” and move
that the legislative Assembly should be
abolished, and things be run on the good old
Indian plan, and when they get old and
apoplectic and red-nosed they will be
described as “veterans,” and bo a curse to
the country at large. For there is hardly a
curse among all the curses under Heaven
which can compare to that which descends
ffpoa the land that is full of “ veterans.”
The military hero who has been drilled
till he is a mere bumptious machine
upon two legs is a picturesque object, but all
the originality has been squeezed out of him
by his tight belt, and he is seldom of any use
afterwards—except for military purposes.
There have been hunchbacks and flabby fat
men among those who have done great deeds
in the ways of peace ; and dwarfs, and
knock-kneed individuals with one eye, and
persons with incurable bow-legs have left
their footprints on the sands of time ; but
the big, stalwart veteran in almost every
case is nothing but a veteran till the end.
• # #
The military device advanced by the St.
James’s Gazette might, as that paper states,
furnish this country with a “reserve of men
who have received the best possible military
training,” and the British correspondent
evidently regards this as an advantage which
cannot fail to be appreciated. Probably he
would be surprised to hear that what Aus-
tralia wants above all things is to avoid
getting such a reserve. In its own insane
fashion it wants to take its chance. A big
army leads to destruction and is the sure
road to ruin. It is used far more often to
crush the democracy at home than to stave
off enemies abroad, and if it gets big enough
it points the way inevitably either to despot-
ism or anarchy. It is the cause of foreign
war ; the majority of campaigns are begun
either because the army is discontented and
must be employed in bloodshed abroad
to postpone trouble at home, or because
some politician is longing for a light and the
existence of a military force gives him the
opportunity to gratify his longings, or
because the country next door is increasing
its army and it is advisable to suppress it
before the increase becomes too formidable,
or because the army is being enlarged, and
the adjacent nation takes the alarm and
declares war to prevent it getting larger. It
is an incubus that keeps on expanding,
because it is the business of each nation to
have a larger force than its neighbour, and
thus the evil has no end. And the experi-
ence of Europe has demonstrated that the
country which has a large force is attacked
much more frequently than that which has
a small one, for the existence of a powerful
military establishment is a challenge to every
adjacent State against which it could possibly
be used, and wars are undertaken quite as
frequently for the sake of wiping out the
aggressive battalions over the frontier as for
the acquisition of territory. Thus it is that
Norway, Sweden, Greece, Portugal, Spain
and Switzerland, with their mere skeleton
armies, stand peacefully outside the chaos of
European politics, while Austria, Germany,
Italy and France, with their armed millions,
live perpetually on the verge of a volcano.
Ibis world is a far more peaceful place
for non-aggressive nations, which attend
strictly to their own business, than the
Indian Office would tain have us
believe. There are even now in Europe
various little States that could barely put
one combatant in the field, which have
lived in comparative quietness for centuries,
and which promise to pursue the even tenor
of their way for centuries more. There is
no visible reason why Australia should not
do the same—so long as she abstains from
fooling with “ reserves,” and growing un-
necessary colonels and veterans and similar
munitions of war, all of which things lead to
disaster as certainly as sunrise leads to dav.
The man who lays in a gun as a peaceful
ornament is seized, sooner or later, with an
insane desire to tire it off and hear the
report. He may wait eighty years before
the madness comes upon him, but either to
him or his sons or his grandsons it inevitably
comes at last.
Boycotting and Intimidation.
Last week Chief Justice Darley, of New South
Wales, sitting in state with the tail of the dead
horse upon his cranium, gave judgment, with
huge costs, against Henry Scott Harden,
solicitor (“re a Gent., one”), whereby that
person is abolished in his legal capacity for one
year with costs, and is ordered after the expira-
tion of that period to remain abolished in-
definitely until these costs are paid in full. The
charge brought against Mr. Harden, and the
views of the Chief Justice thereon, arc fully
expressed in the judgment, as follows ;
The first finding was that the United Mercantile Asso-
ciation of Australasia was not a bona-fide company, a d
that the registering and the carrying on of the company's
business was to enable Hardkx, as solicitor, to mo er
costs for the collection of debts. The Frothonotai v led
found th t this was a mere sham company. The result of
'hat was that .Air. Hardkx for the purpose of gel in*
t >gether a large business—and it was a very large best
ness for so young a practitioner—falsely pretended that
there was such a company, and by this false pretence < b-
tained this large business. He put forth to the public
that there was s tch u company, when, q.s a matter
of fact, there was no such company at all—for he
was the company—and the public were induced to
1 ut this enormous business in the hands of such a young
I raotitioner believing that there was a strong cornnany
I eltind him, and thus the public entrusted him with bine
ness which he could not otherwise have obtained. Tney
found that in six months he issued no less than 300 writs
out of that court, and of these 254 were for sums under
£3O. It was quite true, fortunately, that the public had
not suffered, for the sums obtained from debtors wi re
duly handed over, so that there was no charge of mis-
appropriating against Mr. Hardkx. Taking everything
into consideration—the very large amount of costs which
he would have to pay—because they intended to imnf—
the whole of the costs on him - before he could nrarnif 0
ayam they thought the case would be met by susuenHß
jmn from practice for the term of one year. He vvoniS
have to pay the whole of the costs incurred by the
thonotary, including the shorthand-writer’s fees and n?’
cosls of the Incorporated Law Institute, and at the
of the 12 months he would be further suspended till n,
whole of the cosls were paid. That was to say l te wonm
not be allowed to practise again until the whole of n.o
costs were paid. 1116
* * #
Mr. Harden’s offence, as will be seen by tins
statement, consisted chiefly in the fact that he
was the United Mercantile Association, and the
Association was a concern which recovered
debts and did other legal work at an unusually
low rate—-therefore Mr. Harden committed the
brutal outrage of being a cheap lawyer. There
is no law in Australia to prohibit any man
calling himself an association or a company or a
bank or a joint-stock enterprise of almost any
sort, so long as his business is honestly con-
ducted. Also, there is very little law to
prevent him doing so when his business is dis-
honestly conducted. Almost every Hebraic
money-lender in Australia calls himself a bank
or a loan and finance corporation, and almost
every plain John Smith who opens an office
calls himself Smith & Co., or Smith Brothers
yet even Chief-Justice Harley raises no objec-
tion. Therefore, the United Mercantile
Association tvas no more bogus than the aver-
age, and it is a striking evidence of the rage
which fills the judicial soul at anybody who
cuts down the fees in the legal profession that
Sir Frederick Harley should denounce the
use of this absolutely harmless term as a “ false
pretence.” Moreover, his statement that by
this false pretence “ the public entrusted him
(Harden) with business which he could not
otherwise obtain,” is another surprising proof
of how the bench gets mad at the idea of cheap
law, for Chief-Justice Harley assuredly
couldn’t toll what business Harden would or
would not have been entrusted with, under
conditions that didn’t exist, any more than he
could tell what house would have fallen on him
under circumstances that didn’t happen, or
what would have become of him if his grand-
father hadn’t been born.
* • •
There was not even the slightest attempt
made to prove that anybody—except the guild
of lawyers—lost in any way by Harden’s pro-
ceedmgs, “The public,” as Judge HaliLEy
remarked, “ had not suffered, for the sums
obtained from debtors were duly handed over
so that there was no charge of misappropriating
against" the Unhappy “Gent, one.” These
remarks seem to imply that the Judge was
really surprised because there wasn’t a charge
of misappropriation in the bill, and that a
lawyer who would serve the public at low rates
was so very likely to embezzle his client’s cash
that it was necessary to mention publicly that
he hadn’t done it. At the same time, as nobody
had made the slightest suggestion of fraud, the
remark was altogether uncalled for. If Mr,
Harden or anybody else had risen up, ajmopos
of nothing in particular, and assured the audi-
ence that Chief Justice Harley had never in
all his life been guilty of bigamy or liogThievihg
that gentleman would undoubtedly have raised
Caln about this generous defence "of his charac-
ter. He would, in such a case, have probably
regarded the gratuitous statement that he
wasn t a hog-thief as containing an implication
that he looked very like one, and therefore it
was necessary to offer an explanation on the
subject, and his own superfluous explanation
about Mr. Harden carries precisely the same
construction. It suggested that a man who
furnished law below the usual tariff had neces-
sarily a hang-dog expression about him and was
capable of any enormity. Supplying cheap law
is a cardinal sin among lawyers, and it is
almost surprising that the Judge didn’t also
state that Mr. Harden hadn’t disembowelled
anybody and wasn’t suspected of arson.
* * #
The unfortunate “Gent., one,” being a
lawyer, is compelled to belong to a trades-
union, The trades-union of the law is the
most utterly tyrannous and unscrupulous one
upon the face of the earth. It allows of
no freedom of contract—therefore Harden,
being a lawyer, had to join it or lose his
means of livelihood. The unionist shearer
simply refuses to Work with the shearer who
accepts less than union- rates and does
not conform to union rules. The lawyer
compels other lawyers to belong to his
union, and if one of them shears his
client below the union tariff he not only
refuses to work with him but he hauls him
before a court, gets him fined an enormous sum
in costs, and obtains an order that he shall give
up work for a time—or, perhaps, for ever. If
the non-unionist lawyer is caught shearing a
client, after being convicted of working below
the fixed rates, he gets into other troubles
which may ultimately end in his being con-
signed to a gaol. \V hen the unionist shearer
attempts to coerce the non-unionist shearer into
obeying the rules of his association it is called
boycotting and intimidation and a brutal out-
rage upon the liberties of a free citizen ; when
he compels him to knock off work the troops
and Nordenfeldt guns are called out, and special
constables are enrolled, and there is a howl of
rage throughout the Tory press about the
animal selfishness of the working classes. If any
shearer had done, in the interests of his union,
what the law has just done for the sake of the
lawyers’ trade-union, he would have got three
years’ imprisonment at the very lowest estimate
—Judge Harley himself would probably have
given him seven. As for the idea of making
the non-unionist pay the expenses incurred in
boycotting and intimidating him that idea never
even dawned upon anybody outside the legal
profession—yet Judge Harley has ordered Mr.
Harden to pay costs, which will probably amount
to much over £lOOO, incurred in boycotting him,
and never even turned a hair in the process.
Why the whole legal system of the country
should be at the service of the lawyer to
enable him to enforce the rules of his miserable,
grasping union, while the Bench would have a
cataleptic fit if asked to lend a similar support
to the guild of shearers when it wants to sup-
press a blackleg, is still another mystery and
one that is too vast for present consideration.
* * #
It is a curious circumstance that the Chief


Justice seemed to regard Mr, Harden’s success
jn his profession as a personal injury, and as an
aggravation of his sins. He had “ a very large
business for so young a practitioner,” said Sir
Frederick in one place, and a moment later
he complained that “ the public were induced
to put this enormous business in the hands of
such a young practitioner,” Evidently the
feeling was that Mr. Harden was turning out
too much law per annum, and this was a grie-
vance which required to be stopped, lest prices
should go down. Now and then the collier
complains in like manner that certain mines are
turning out too much coal, and thereby reducing
prices, which, as the inevitable result, leads to
a, reduction of wages, and he also takes
measures to stop the same grievance. Then he
is described in the papers as a brutal ignoramus,
and troops and police are sent to run him
in lest he should burst into riot and
lead to destruction of property. Yet the
Chief Justice goes on exactly the same
track without arousing any military or other
interference, and the man Avho turned out too
much law is suspended for 12 months, in order
to keep up the price of law, and condemned to
pay costs. If the miners’ trades union, under
similar circumstances, ordered that the mine-
owner should be suspended for 12 months,
even without costs, to keep up the price of
fuel, all heaven and earth would not contain
the row that would ensue. Why all these
things are, The Bulletin does not attempt to
explain ; the one thing that is certain among
many uncertainties is that Mr. Harden comes
very badly out of the complication. The charge
against him is that he called himself an Associa-
tion —which is an act that is prohibited by no
statute now extant, that he was not so brutally
greedy as is required by the rules of the trades-
union, and that he was strictly honest in his deal-
ings with his clients ; and the sentence of the
court is that he shall be boycotted and intimi-
dated for the space of one year, that he shall
be prevented from following his lawful occupa-
tion ; and that he shall pay a colossal sum to
the people who boycotted and intimidated him.
All honest lawyers whose bills of costs aren’t
sufficiently exorbitant will do well to take
warning by this result. If they do not, a
Nemesis in horse-hair is upon their track.
Concerning Birth and Beer.
Lady Henry Somerset, a well-connected person
with a holy war-cry —“ no fallen women and no
drunkards ” —has climbed up her family tree to
make a few remarks on the modern manufac-
ture of nobility. Spake the haughty teeto-
talless
To-day the man who brewed the strongest XX, and the
gin-distiller who could place on the market more than
anybody else of a spirit he would probably never dare to
put on his own table—these men were raised to be the
perpetual legislators of the country. The British peerage
was becoming the British beerage (chestnut!), and what
used to be the blue blood of Verb de Ybre was changing
to the fiery liquid of Beer de Beer.
The noble philanthropist gives herself away in
the latter clause of her indictment. As long as
she sticks to the fact that the Queen delights
to exalt the fat trader who is remarkable for
ready cash, and that the grog-industry, being
based upon a world-tvide weakness, is one of
the most lucrative that an ordinary money-
maker can wallow in—as long as she confines
herself to this hideous truth, Lady Henry
Somerset is as right as right can be. But
when she commences to drivel concerning
the gore of the Veres de Vere and insinuates
that hereditary legislation should be the prero-
gative of families similar to her own, this
respectable woman becomes a dangerous
nuisance. If her blessed mission enables her to
obtain a respectful hearing for these painful
ideas on the peerage question, it were better
for the drunk and the fallen to fall and be
drunk as before without her noble interference.
The morality cause can dispense with her sup-
port on such terms. Democrat John Burns is
checking drunkenness and degradation by
exhorting his humble friends to wash their
faces and work steadily for a political system in
which the difference between a belted earl of
long descent and a brand-new bloated brewer
will be merely a historic conundrum. As a
crusader, Lady Somerset is of no more practical
benefit to mankind than Richard the First
was. In his early idiotic way the monarch
went to a lot of trouble to push Christianity.
Furthermore, he believed very hard in coming
over with the Conqueror and would have
spurned the thought of conferring a barony
upon the man who made his beer.
* * *
Lady Henry Somerset’s prattle is so essen-
tially the prattle of middle-class Toryism
(which loves the name of Nobility, but regrets
that it can’t all be ancient from the start) that
she is worth throwing a little ink at. More-
over, she happens, for a certain family reason,
to be a specially appropriate mark. Once again,
then, The Bulletin rises to mention that the
sinfulness of the Peerage as an institution is not
increased by the inclusion of a brewer any more
than it is i-educed by the ennobling of a Poet
Laureate. No man makes his own ancestor,
and the great-grandson of a virtuous genius is
no better qualified to be a power in the land
than the lineal descendant of an ill-conducted
pawnbroker. For founding an old family and
instigating a strain of blue blood a pawnbroker
does quite as well as anybody else. He is
neither better nor worse than a brewer, or a
brilliant field-marshal, or a Lord Chancellor.
■St. Leonards and the late noodle Cairns were
both of them heirs of lawyers who sat on the
woolsack. The one got into trouble for
assaulting a parlourmaid, and the other
achieved notoriety by jilting a chorus-girl
and being “pulled” for breach-of-promise.
It is fair to suppose that the eldest son of the
original Vere dh Vere (assuming the fact of
such a person having been) was not a stronger
argument in favour of “perpetual legislation
in Ins male line than his successor of to-day.
I’robably Vere de Vere No. 1 was a cut-
throat. Certainly he was an ignorant barbarian
as compared with a modern brewer, and why
his blood should have improved in 500 years
any more than Bung’s blood can improve in a
like period is a point which only the totally-
abstaining daughter-in-law of a duke can ex-
plain. And if both strains of blood get worse
as they grow blue the relative position of the
two ancestors remains as before- Lady Henry
Somerset is a genteel ass. The very name she
bears is a shocking reminder of the fact that if
“blood’’can claim to confer any sort of dis-
tinction upon a family, the distinction may be
something to blush for.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Shareholder’s Troubles.
A decision recently arrived at by Judge
Hamilton, in Melbourne, opens a gratifying
prospect for Australian shareholders. Some
four or live years ago a man named Bald owned
some shares in the City and County Bank, and
in October, 1888, he sold them in the accustomed
way through a broker. The purchaser was the
manager of the bank, and he bought —so he
declares—on account of the bank, which was
then engaged in creating an artificial demand
for its own shares in order that it might rush off
a new issue at a premium. There is no reason
to believe that Bald knew who bought the
shares, and in such cases the seller very seldom
does know ; he doesn’t even see the signature
at the foot of the buyer’s cheque, for
the broker puts that document to his
own credit, and gives his own cheque to the
seller. Bald simply got rid of his shares, and
thought no more about them. But when the
City and County Bank burst, it was discovered
that the transfer had never been registered —■
which was entirely the purchaser’s fault, for
the seller has no well-defined powers to compel
the other man to register. Then the liquidator
dropped upon the miserable Bald for calls to
help pay the liabilities of the defunct institu-
tion, and Judge Hamilton decided that he
must pay, because the purchase by the Bank was
an illegal transaction, and was therefore null
and void, or something to that effect. Conse-
quently, Bald is fined in an indefinite amount,
under the name of calls, because another person
committed an illegal action, and the man who
did the illegal action isn’t fined at all. Of
course, as the sale was illegal, the person who
collected the dividends after 1888 collected them
illegally, but Bald doesn’t recover this item —■
lie cast his scrip upon the waters, and the loss
came back after many days, but the profits are
never heard of any more. Under this system
it is a precarious job to be a shareholder in Aus-
tralia. No man who has ever sold shares can
tell for certain whether he sold them legally or
not ; all he can do is to ask the broker, and the
chance of the broker telling him the truth is
exceedingly limited, for the fraternity arc among
the hardest’ liars of the community and have
been so from the days of Ananias down-
wards. And, as the natural result, any
man who ever owned an interest in a
wild-cat mine or a few shares in a bank is
liable to be visited by the ghost of his old
speculation in after years, or if he dies before
it comes, it may arrive in time to ruin his
widow or his children. The ex-shareholder, in
fact, is a Frankenstein Avhose monster is
always waiting for a chance to come back and
step on him in his old age, and flatten him out.
When once a man has owned shares in anything
his only security, apparently, is to fly to the
uttermost ends of the earth, and lie low there
till his company bursts, and the liquidation is
over, and the liquidator is dead, and everything
is wound up and forgotten.
Tkk “National” Association of New South
Wales decided at its last meeting to call together
“representatives of the cricket, rowing, foot-
ball and other clubs, and the various organisa-
tions of young men in Sydney, with a view to
inducing their members to take a greater
interest in public matters and become members
of the Association.” This means that in each
club some member will be put up to move
“ that this club gives a solid and unanimous
support to the Association,” and as all that
section of the calf-and-breast-bone brigade,
which approves of the movement will be pre-
sent, and a lot of the others will be absent, the
resolution stands a reasonable show, in many
instances, of being passed. Then, if the thing
can be fixed properly, it will become one of
the recognised qualifications of an athlete that
he must be a bigoted Tory, and the block vote
of the man who kicks the inflated football, and
the man who hangs out on the weather gun-
wale when a gale is blowing, and the “ harrier”
Avho goes out on Saturday afternoons and gallops
through Chinamen’s gardens, will, in a great
measure, run the country. The idea, in short,
is to arrange a device by which, when an elec-
tion comes on, the “ National ” Association will
select the candidates, and send out its ukase to
the cricketer and footballer and the boating-
man “ mid nodings on,” and the harrier,
and the affiliated pony - racing man, and
the Y.M.C.A., and the society that collects
old postage stamps, and the Society for the Pre-
vention of Cruelty to Animals, and the Charity
Organisation Society, and the Liedertafel,
and the Cremation Society, and the chess-club,
and the dog-show, and command their unani-
mous support with no questions asked. It is a
great device, but then it will only end in the
democratic athletes starting a separate business
on their own account, and if one fellow hangs
out on the jibboom in the cause of proputty,
another fellow will hang out on another jibboom
in the interests of manhood suffrage, and when
one man does a high jump for the cause of
Toryism, another will jump pretty nearly as
high on the other side. The “ National ” Ass.,
as he grows older, becomes more of an ass than
ever.
Editor Bulletin, —Sir, —A series of blunders
has led Australia into her present position with
regard to the British money-lender. A country
which can grow all her own beef and bread,
raise all the “ claret, champagne, and sardines ”
that she requires, and produce even her own
quinine and castor - oil, ought never to be
“beholden” to foreign aid or soil for food,
clothing, medicine, or luxuries, every one of
which, even to the very gold and silver required
for current coin, can be yielded from her own
soil and w'aters. Yet, what do we see? There
is a place called Dundee, in Scotland, whence
they have to send 3000 miles for lemons, and
5000 miles for sugar, and yet these people make
marmalade, and send it 12,000 miles to Austra-
lian consumers, who, it they like, can grow the
lemons and the sugar in their ow'n back
gardens ! Ex pede Herculetn. Here is a clue
to the whole business. Australia, Avho, if she
were only true to herself, could be a lender in
place of a borrower of money, gets loans from
Great Britain, not all in money, but partly in
steel rails and the like, on which she has to pay
interest for ever. It just reminds one of the
other class of money-lender, who advances to
the spendthrift £IOO, of which £ls are in
sovereigns and the balance of £BS in “ wine ”
and “pictures.” It is much of a piece and is a
disastrous job for the borrower in both cases ;
for the steel rails go to make railways to the
Never Never, lines Avhich don’t pay for the
axle-grease, and were only undertaken to retain
the political votes of insolvent paid-members of
Parliament. What a use for a ucav and glori-
ous country, its funds and its credit! Were
Australia of my temper, she would, at all
sacrifice, in the way of land sales, Treasury
bills and other “ tide-over” measures, in future
let England’s money congest for ever in her
coffers, or be lent to those “ wild cat ” South
American repAiblics which the venal, black-
mailing London “financial” press so delights
to honour.—Yours, etc., N. Bartley, Brisbane.

the recent cockcroAving assertions of the Tory
papers that the working men in the Burnett
district are in favour of Kanaka labour. Bun-
daberg is the capital of the Musgrave elector-
ate which recently re-elected W. B. O’Connell.
And Bundaberg lias reversed Musgrave’s fat.
Hall, who polled 450, was opposed by Kanako-
phile Duffy (352), Curtis (169), and Skyrtng
(14). Duffy is one of that new sort of Tory
uncommonly too frequent in Australia. Time
was, within the writer’s recollection, when
Michael Duffy rowed by night and day the
ferry-boat at Gillon’s Crossing— ls bob a Aveek
and tucker, and camp in the boat-shed. He
loudly professed Home Rule and labour views,
and w T as of frugal mind, but also acquisi-
tive. His hunger was for land, and he
became one of Bundaberg’s wealthiest citizens,
was several times Mayor, and wrote a much-
advertised testimonial as to the efficacy of (say)
“ Horner’s Unsafe Killall.” When Avealthy,
the ex-land-hankerer attached himself to the
MTlwraith party, Mac, in the meantime,
having taken Gin-Gin station in that district.
Duffy was the mightiest political possibility
for Bundaberg of this election. He blames
Curtis, a storekeeper of much local renown,
for splitting up his chances. Had Duffy polled
all Curtis’s votes (and he Avould not), he Avould
then only have scored 71 ahead of the despised
Labour and Anti-Kanaka man. Skyring’s 14
need not be counted, and yet Skyring is one of
the pioneers of Bundaberg, and was an in-
fluential and Avell-to-do butcher in the days
Avhen boatman Mike slept in his shirt, pants
and bare feet on the form of Gillon’s ferry-
boathouse. Politics is queer business.
At the meeting of the Ncav South Wales bogus
“ National ” Association last week—
A lengthy debate ensued as t o the best means by which
the present depression might lie checked, and a similar
State of things avoided in the future. The general con-
sensus of opinion was that the difficulty had arisen from
the too great congestion of population in the cities, which
could only be relieved by the proper settlement of the
people upon the land. Various suggestions for bringh g
about an improved state of affairs were put forward, but
as there appeared to be a great diversity of opinion upon
the matter it was proposed by the president that c< m-
peiitixe essavs be invited, and that a prize lie offered tor
the best composition setting forth a means of placing a
greater proportion of the population upon the soil, with
the. least passible injury to exinlin;/ ritjhU. This pro-
posal was unanimously agreed to, and a member of the
council present offered a prize of £25, to be given to the
successful essayist.
The Bulletin hereby furnishes the outlines of
a scheme that would annex that £25 and give
the utmost satisfaction to the “ National ” Ass.
Let every squatter in New South Wales ha
granted the freehold of liis run on condition
that he leases out half the property in 200-
acre farms for the benefit of the unemployed.
The squatter to decide which half shall he
leased. In order to give an aspect of
benevolence to the scheme let each tenant
have his ground free for five years on con-
dition that he effects improvements to the
extent of (say) £3 an acre. After the five years
is up let the landlord, the squatter, fix the rent
as he thinks best, and if it isn’t paid, let him
grab the improvements. In order to facilitate
settlement, let railways and irrigation-works
be constructed, and paid for by a special tax
upon the tenant. This item is not, however, to
be a mortgage upon the improvements. Also,
let all the unemployed who are not thus pro-
vided for be arrested and set to making roads
and tanks on the squatters’ own half of the
property, and let all shearing be done by
Kanaka labour, so as to obviate strikes, which
are disastrous and lead to vexation. Any
person suspected of firing grass to be hanged.
Each squatter to be appointed a magistrate,
with the powers of a District Court Judge, in
his own district, and be allowed to swear-in
special constables and to flog by summary juris-
diction. Every tenant who is evicted and loses
his improvements through non-payment of rent
to be moved on westward, and planted on a
farm on some other run, and set to wprk to
make more improvements. If this scheme
wouldn’t “settle the people on the land,” what
kind of a scheme would ?

The Parson He Says “No.”
Bishop Webber: “ I fail to see it is a bad thing to bring
the islanders face to face with the more trying conditions
of our unaccustomed (sic) civilization.” Contrast the
utterances of the Anglican shepherd with (he manly and
stirring appeal issued to White Queensland by Rev. J.
Moore, of Bundaberg !
We thought to raise a nation hero of men who
dared be free;
We thought to have one people here, right through
from sea to sea ;
We thought to clear the slavery taint from Queens-
land, high and low,
We thought our aim a noble one —but Parson, he
says, “ No !”
We thought it wrong to steal away the black man
from his isle ;
We thought the slave-trade dirty, and we thought
the slaver vile;
We thought the words of waning—“Ye shall
reap just as ye sow ” —
Would bring us home a harvest black —but Parson,
he says, “No !”
We thought the taint of blood accursed that rested
on the trade ;
We knew the “Jason’s ” story well—the trip the
“ Hopeful ” made;
We nourished hopes wc soon should say, “It
happened long ago—
Thank Hod, ’tis o’er—the dirty game’’-but
Parson, he says, “No !”
We thought that all men hated the hideous social
sore—
Bred by the rotten traffic—that festers at our
door;
The thousand graves of sable slaves cried, “Let
our brethren go !”
But a traitor band have sold the land and say, like
Parson, “No !”
We thought of Wilberforce and Sharpe, we
thought of Manning, too ;
We thought our cause must win applause f;om all
the good and true ;
We thought the Church might lend her hand to
raise the child of woe,
As oft she did in days of yore—but Parson, lie
says, “No!”
We hoped—in vain our hoping. We see the
hireling stand
And preach the cause of cruel wrong as Gospel in
the land :
Ye followers of the gentle Christ—come, tell us,
is it so ?
this the creed of the Nazarene? Hie People
thunder—“ No !”
Mb. Morgan.

William, of Woolloomooloo.
Dear Bill, ’ere goes a letter (which I 'ope 'll find
yer “ fit ”)
On a matter which ’as shook my bloomin’ liver up
a bit.
Have yer ’card about our Railways and the way
as ’ow the State
Has been blowin’ cold and hot upon that ever-

He was biz’nesslike and clever, and a covey they

Till every country member ’ad a station near his

At the orfulness of Speight in makin’ lines that

oughter ’ave the rim.

’is place,

Gives Speight a noo engagem nt for another
blessed term.
The Railways kep’ on losin’ wnss than ever all the

For the place was going broker and lie couldn’t

To increase the population on them lines that
didn’t pay.
All “ at once ” he got suspended, as election time

If he doesn’t know his bizness we must ’ave a man

Then Wheeler undertook to show ’ow Speight ’ad
made a mess,
He raised the fares, and snorted when he found
the traffik less;
He prayed the Lord to ’elp ’im, so I’ve ’card a

But the more he worked the Railways, why the
more they didn't pay.
And Speight, who was so bad they couldn’t stand
’im any more,
Will take six thousand quid (becos the country’s
very poor)
In return for his consentin’ not to offer a
“defence” —
Which explains the situashun to a chap with any
sense.
Dear Bill, if yer imployer thort yer wasn’t worth
yer sorlt,
If yer didn’t pay attenshun when he told yer of a
forlt,

There’s more than meets the hoplic in this little

And it don’t refleck much credit on the morals of

For a Govingment whose motto is Retrenchment

I should call this conduct cool, Bill, if it wasn't

816 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, June 15,1892.
My Dear Mooraiunua,—
Two women only, Mrs. 8.8. and Isabel “ Urkert,”
bring careful dressing to bear on “Fedora” at Sydney
Royal, but you can t help wondering whether, in real
life, a woman so mentally worried as Fedora was would
have found time to curl even the short hairs on the naue
of her neck ? Mrs. Beere’s Fedora frocks are the liveliest
she has yet sported. The first has a silver-coloured tail
of velvet coming from the shoulder, the undress of white
satin with dot circles in steel, steel fringing on the cor-
sage, white feather boa, and white plume fan. In act 2,
Miss Urquhart displayed her fine figure in a black silk
bodice and voluminous train with bunches of blue ostrich
on one side ; skirt of pale-blue erSpon with clusters here
and there of black feathers ; big black flirting fan. Mrs.
Beere chose a salad-coloured Empire dress, and the
style didn’t suit her back view. When a lofty lady wears
her waist under her arm-pits she reminds me somehow of
the pantomime giantess who is one man perched on
another’s shoulders. But the gown was very smart —a
green silk, gold ’broidery, yellow satin, and
diamonds.
Mrs. Beere, however, looked her very best in a pale-blue
crepon tea-gown, eccentrically diversified with fine white
lace and scarves of new pink—girdle of pink with golden
ends, worn in the third act, where Otho Stuart (who is
the same clean-shaved, white-cuffed lover whatever he
plays—why doesn’t he take a hint from Mens. Marius,
who has to speak before he is recognised in each bill) is
such a long time accepting an invitation that any other
man would jump at. In the last agony'-scene, Isabel
Urkert, in a wondrous mushroom-coloured frock of
phenomenal fit, and trimmed with brown velvet and
wheat sheaves (in sepia tints) upside down about her
person, gabbled so that she might have been unburdening
her stage-soul in Choctaw—but it didn’t matter much.
\\ hen a fine woman has a well-gored skirt on, the spec-
tacle is more optical than auriculaw. Mrs. Beere pizened
herself in flowing white robes, like the things the Heavenly
choir-girls are said to wear—perhaps Fedora tried it on to
see if angelic millinery beetle her style of beauty before
she let Gallagher go.
The circle was rather erratic in its behaviour at the
diagnosed extensively above. One lady, seated
in deep gloom, stood up and vb.’ently waved her fan at
the actress after each curtain, as if she were a mosquito
ora fly, or something that had to be blown away, but i 5
was only another way of cheering, I suppose. And after
you got used to the upward jerks of this excited patroness
of the drama, in came a nerveful young man with a cup
of very hot coffee for a lady in the front row. It was the
first cup of anything hot 1 have ever seen in the circle.
As a rule the thirsty d.e. seraph dips her flushed visage
into a long, cold, soda-soaked squash, or else a flabby
“ lemonade with ” gets into her nose and makes her eyes
water, so you can imagine how cheerful that pioneer cup
of coffee looked ! If they could manage to have ice-cream
in the summer and coffee in the winter, at the theatres, it
would be—well, for one thing it would be only the most
flnancially-solid young man who could manage to take his
lady friends to the play I suppose.
And, talking about refreshment In dress-circles, I was
rather horrified at the Criterion, the other night, when a
lady in front of me produced an immense sweet from her
handkerchief, sucked it affectionately for five minutes by
the Waterbury, and put it back into her handker-
chief. The staying powers qf that bull’s-eye, or whatever
it was, must have been immense, for she repeated the per-
formance after every act! But let’s be charitable; perhaps
she was cutting her wisdom teeth on it.
The circle was large on the fourth night. Rather a
theatrical design was sported by a young lady—the low-
cut bodice was outlined into a large heart frontally, by
golden fringe. If daws peck at a heart on your sleeve
what’s going to happen when it covers the whole of your
diaphragm? Another lady was stout, and very V-shaped,
of course, but what riveted my astonished eye was when
she guided the handle of her fan half-way down her spine
and agitated it d la scratchback with a bland expression
tike that of a cat basking in the sun in a dairy.
The mid-winter meeting of the A.J.C. was galloped off
at Randwick last Saturday, amid bookmakers’ shrieks
that could be heard half-way across the Centennial Paris.
Belledorn wasn’t largely represented on the stand—none
of the coaches had any women on top, and the throng of
mere men adorning the roofs and the run home gave the
dashing vehicles a misty resemblance to a returning
auction-sale. Nevertheless, two or three satisfactory frocks
were visible.
Mrs. W. Mitchell 1 promenaded in a rough black woollen,
contrasted with sapphire-blue silk, covered on the bodice
with black guipure, bonnet en suite, with flesh-pink
wings;, Mrs. G.eo. Brock wore an uncommon get-up of
pale and dark-blue striped floral brocade, trimmed with
dark-blue ostrich feathers, hat en suite. Miss Carrie
Swain’s well-fitting dress was of navy-blue cloth, Eton
jacket, edged with gimp, over a full blouse vest of del-
blue surah, equestrienne hat of blue velvet, trimmed with
pale-blue ribbon and quills ; a sassiety lady in grey, with
half her sleeves tan from the elbow down, reminded me of
a coffee-coloured sister with her sleeves turned up ; Isabel
Urquhart was startling in white silk, trimmed with black
jet, small horned bonnet en suite ; one of Sydney’s infre-
quent lady doctors wore her scientific little fists poked
mannishly into the deep pockets of a rather unbecoming
sac coat, and another charming girl wanned her digital
extremities in what was probably not the only muff on
the lawn.
Two good concerts eventuated in Sydney last week.
The first (in the Y.M.C.A.) was given by French musicians
iij aid of distressed compatriots, and was a great success
iii every way. Among the instrumentalists Madame Keller..
mann, in cream brocade, played her sparkling valse de
concert with much effect and was overwhelmed with
flowers; Kowalski’s rendering of his own thundering
“Cuirassiers” was a dashing effort, but he began so
boisterously in “The Fairies’ Dance” that it almost
seemed as if the fairies were hopping in clogs. Wiegand,
Dr. Laure, and his son (who is an amateur fiddler), Miss
Young, Mdme. Acngenheyster, Poussard, ai d Mons.
Deslouis filled the bill. The last-named artist was in
splendid voice for his three numbers, especially in the
“ Ballade ” from “ L’Africaine ” and the ever-green “ Les
Hameaux,” with piano, fiddle, and organ support, which
was greatly applauded. By the way, why will Sydney
audiences begin to clap before the singer is finished ? It is
the very worst taste to applaud while the accompanist i 8
playing the concluding bars, for although the song is the
thing there really ought to be a little courtesy shown to
the man at the wheel, so to speak. Snazelle was the only
artist I ever saw equal to the occasion. No matter if the
“front” howled at him at a concert he never moved a
muscle in acknowledgment until the music stopped.
Herr Josef Kretschmann’s pupils’ concert went off
beautifully in the Centennial Hall last Saturday, and
there was a huge audience. The bill was awesomely
classical - Liszt, Spohr, Mendelssohn, etc. —and Signora
Dink, “Charlie” Huenerbein and the Orpheus folks
assisted the pupils (who didn’t seem to exactly cry out
for assistance, by the way) in their little set to with music,
Heavenly maid. Unlike most pupils’ concerts where the
items cover four sides of the programme and you wish
you were dead or next the door, the performers were only
seven in number. Herr Kretschmann is to be congratu-
lated on his good sense in only turning his best pupils
loose on a long-suffering public.
Talking of music reminds me that somebody who is to
astonish Australia directly with the results of her musical
studies in Europe, returned quietly and under an assumed
name to her native city a little while ago. Her old
teacher—warm-hearted, unselfish old man ! who had first
discovered and encouraged her musical talents and given
her lessons “for love,” as they say—was still where she
had left him, but she did not look him up—only casually
happened across him in the street one day. He was filled
with simple delight at the sight of his clever pupil, and
warmly invited her to come and see him—but my lady
drew herself up stiffly: “ Herr Blank,” she said patronis-
ingly, “I am at home every Tuesday!” Now, the
question is how much warm, spontaneous soul will the
lady put into her performances ?
Sydney Women’s Literary Club members have at last
achieved an ark of their ow r n, instead of perching here
and there in barren and undraped rooms lent by Messrs.
So-and-So—and now we shall see whether the fact of
meeting a la Israelite is the reason for the absence of the
milk in the cocoanut. When the art member has splashed
gold paint in impressionist blobs on the door-panels, and
the musical member has a piano in the corner and a fiddle
on top of it, and the aesthetic member has draped every,
thing with giraffe’s-sneeze silk, and the reciting member
hds hired two fat men to try the platform, and the greedy
member has arranged for five o’clock tea to emerge piping
hot from some unsuspected direction, and the lazy mem-
ber has seen to the chairs—then will the hour be, and let
us hope the woman. But, at present, the ink isn’t dry
on their first rent-receipt.
Brisbane, by the way, has a Literary Club. It is ad-
dicted to water-picnics, where the members read one
another’s little efforts aloud, and the rest —except the
genius whose thunder is in the air—murmur ecstatic
nothings at the finish. But what fearful opportunities
for retaliation the system offers to the jealous poetess to
spoil the effect of a rival’s “ little thing !” A judiciously-
timed hiccough on the reader’s part would ruin the effect
of an epic. In Sydney and Melbourne every girl clutches
her own MS., you bet.
Sydney Women’s College Fund continues to be a drag
on its unhappy backers, who are now too jaded to travel
round with the bag—or so one judges from the fact that
£lO9 Is. 6d. was all the treasurer had in hand “for
general purposes” at the recent committee meeting. The
Women’s College was established by sheer (ladylike)
obstinacy and determination, and no doubt its energetic
foundresses thought that when the educational ark was
a-moverin’ they might relax a little—sit in the shade and
admire their fell work, as it were, but alas 1 none of the
enthusiasts who started it have really got out of collar
since. There are more shows got up for this white
elephant of book-larnin’ than were ever needed to keep
any institution in Sydney on its feet before.
What becomes of the beautiful wire-stalked blossoms
that are showered on friends leaving for England per
mail-steamer? I have often seen a something in the
veiled eye of the priestess of mal-de-mer which seemed to
say that she wasn’t goin’ to have no muddle in her cabins,
and the other day my suspicions, were confirmed. Some-
body wrote from Adelaide : “ I was very sick the first day
and the stewardess threw all my lovely flowers into the
sea.”
Talking of life on the brackish, a ship’s doctor (mind
you, he had a Byronic eye and stood six feet in any of his
numerous crewelled slippers, so, perhaps, he had an
unusually warm time of it) told me that the women on
board a mail-steamer, when they can't think of
any other complaint to gnaw at their vitals always
fall back on hysterics. They are nearly always
mock hysterics, and the patient doesn’t drum, her
heels on the floor very much if her feet are Australian
size; nor does she give out the hysterical laugh if her
back-teeth are gold-stopped, and so on, right through the
ladies’ cabins. So a new complaint has arisen called
“ Ship’s hysterics,” which is when the poor dear looks
slyly out of the corner of her eye to see what effect her
sufferings are having upon the doctor.
Which reminds me that there is rather a dearth of
gold buttons in Sydney lately, and though the dear
girls may regret the scarcity of Captain Corcoran et
cie, it’s scarcely likely their brothers will. Because men
do say that the gallant tars will junket to any extent, but
leave the settling-up to civilians. As everybody knows,
when a little party is made up to go picnicking in a hired
drag it’s the rule for the men of the party to go shares in
the thing and make it lighter for all pockets ; but the
marine individual follows the ladies in this as in every
other individual instance and ignores waiters, grooms,
toll-bars, and “ the piper” with lady-like indifference to
everything except the lovely view. Therefore, he isn’t
half so popular with Qussie as he is with Angelina. You
see, he doesn’t waltz with Gussie.
1 am requested to announce the engagement of Miss
Jessie Stevenson, of Meridale, South Yarra (Vic.), to Mr.
Geo. Turnball, manager of the National Bank, Sydney.
After which it seems apropos to say that 1 would have
enjoyed “Engaged” at the Criterion a great deal more
than I did if the adventures of Mr. Cheviot Hill, who
adores every woman he meets, hadn’t reminded me of
Deeming’s little fads. As Cheviot made furious love to
three seraphs you couldn’t help picturing him planting
some of them under the hearthstone. The dressing wasn’t
exceptionally lovely in Gilbert’s comedy, and my eagle
eye delected resurrections everywhere. For instance,
Pattie B.’s bridal dress of white brocade was the identical
staggerer that Miss Temple wore when she was married in
“ School ” ; and Miss Browne’s bit of blue muslin in the
last act rose up like an old, old friend.
On dit that Lady Jersey has made all necessary
arrangements tor a trip to Samoa, hut when or how isn’t
revealed—let’s hope anyway that she will wait until th
winter season of alleged gaieties stop. What globe-
trotters the Jerseys are !
In a rag-shop the other day I overheard a question and
answer between what was revealed to be a brand-new
bride and a female friend, which exhibited those very
general ceremonies—hasty marriages—in such a bony
light that the impatientßenedict of future connubialisings
may possibly be in less of a hurry to annex his Beatrice—
when 1 have told him all about it. Said tin friend to the
bride —who was seated at the counter with all sorts of
necessaries strewn round her—“ What! shopping already ?”
to which the bride made answer with a smile I fancied
rather deep: “ My dear, you must remember I had only
just a fortnight to get ready in ! ” and her reply set me
thinking of the manifestly economical aspect a spot
wedding has for the blushing bride. Can Petruchio
blame his Katharine (whom he has raced up to the altar)
if he finds that his angel was too rushed to lay in a dozen
pairs of silken hose and goodness knows what in lingerie?
Can he reasonably complain (when he remembers how he
broke the record round to the parson) if, instead of his
Poppet coming to cheer his hearthstone with a wardrohe-
full of wearing apparel paid for by Paw, he has to lit her
out cap a pic and foot it all out after the honeymoon ? if
the young man about to marry takes my advice he will
give his Pansy Blossom as much breathing lime as she
pretends to want, for it can’t be encouraging to commence
your double life with a darling who cries out for clothing
from the matrimonial jump.
There was a race-meeting for amateur riders at Rand-
vviek the other Wednesday, and the expected happened
as usual—half the silk-clad horsemen fell off. Neither
riders nor mounts were exceedingly beautiful viewed in
the lurid light that beats about a race-horse, and one
enthusiast took to the track on a subdued and melancholy
sort of combination of hoofs and hair whose daily avoca-
tion is to drag his tormentor all over the place in a buggy.
1 don’t like race-meetings for amateurs. When the starter
drops his flag and the inexperienced riders scuffle off in a
bunch, it hurts one’s sympathetic soul to see some
woman s face go while as chalk as this or that horse
makes a mistake at the first hurdle.
Madame Melba seems to be giving Australian women
big ad. in the “cities of an effete civilisation,” for I read
that at the Nice carnival her carriage, with a white swan
on either side of the coachman’s seat, was among the
prettiest there, and created a great sensation. She is also
reported to have worn “a very distingud toilet ” of grey
and gold—so if people think Australia is femininely popu-
lated with black gins in shark’s-teeth necklaces after that
the intelligence of Yewrope is past praying for.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The Maoriland papers got no cables re the
Jackson-Slavin fight. The Press Association
decided some time back to take no details of prize-
fights, and their agents went one better and sent
no result of the big scrapping-match.

At last the Sydney licensing bench un-
compromisingly begins to knock out the dirty
rum-mill which, though licensed as “ hotel,” never
supplies a meal, and does not contain a decent
bed, but fattens all the time on the diseased
appetite of the blue-nosed soaker. Seventy per
cent of the so called Sydney “hotels” never would
be missed.
A Scotch clergyman announces, as the
result of many years’ observation, that ;?0,000
men and women go to bed drunk, in Glasgow,
every Saturday night. The calculation looks
eminently probable—in fact, anybody who has put
in a Saturday night in Gorbals, or Bridgeton, or
any other locality when the working-men most do
congregate, would probably reckon it an under-
estimate. The top storey of a tenement house is
the best place to gather information upon the sub-
ject, and the best time to begin is about ten p.m.,
when the drunk comes marching home and starts
to raise Cain. There are probably a dozen house-
holders living in the one tenement —sometimes
the houses contain only one room apiece, and in
that case there may be twenty or more— and out
of the twelve, seven are probably drunk. Some of
them leave their work at two, and are hopelessly
tight by four, and get home and break all the
dishes and whale the wife and the family by five,
and then sleep till seven, and go out again and
are beastly drunk a second time before nine ; but
the majority get “slewed” slowly, and arrive
home just about ten. Then they begin to arouse
Gehenna on the stairs. There is generally one
man who always collapses on the bottom step,
and after singing one or two verses of “Scots wha
hae, &c, , ” he falls asleep and the other people
step on him. Then there is the other man who
never can remember whether he lives up two
stairs or three, and he kicks furiously at
the wrong door, and the owner of the place
either rushes out and fights him on the landing,
or else embraces him and they both sing
“Auld Lang Syne.” Also there is the
man whose wife always gets drunk on credit on
Saturday afternoon, and liquidates the bill on
Monday, and she usually locks her husband out,
and they vituperate through the keyhole while the
old man is picking the lock. As for the other
drunks they are various, including the man who
sees a snake on the staircase, and the two indi-
viduals who wake up at midnight and yell till
two a.m. Most of the women get their clothes
out of pawn on Saturday evening, provided the
head of the family arrives home in time, and put
them in pawn again on Monday morning ; and
the fat woman with the clever children goes
down in the evening and pays five shillings off
the bill at the grocery shop which is lighted up by
two candles that gutter down on the cheese. Also
the woman whose husband has drunk all his
wages before he got home pervades the building
trying to borrow some coals, and her daughter
with the naked feet rushes round the adjacent
shops trying to get some oatmeal on credit. Last
of all, in the sma’ oors of the morning, the tene-
ment is wakened up by two policemen trying to
find the man who struck Buckley at the public-
house round the corner, and after they are gone
there is a great calm till Sunday morning, when
all the householders wake up and wash themselves
at the sink, and look wretchedly out of the win-
dow, or else lie in bed and smoke or chew tobacco.
The present writer sat op the corpse of one man
who used to chew tobacco almost all night, and
expectorate backwards over his head, but he wasn't
Scotch. He died suddenly at last, partly through
the visitation of Providence and partly through
the tobacco juice getting into'his eye.

Ab Sydney Royal, in the middle of last
week, Mrs, Bernard-Beere appeared with a strong
support in Sardou’s drawing-room melo-drama of
“Fedora,” English version, and played it with
varying success up to Wednesday last, when
it was withdrawn in favour of “ London
Assurance,” with the star in the part of Lady
Gay Spanker. There is little need to recapitulate
the plot of the piece written by Sardou to accom-
modate the histrionic needs of Sara Bernhardt. It
is simply the story of a woman who in her lust
for vengeance sacrifices all that is near and
dear to the victim whom she has learned
to love, and who, in the last act, in
a passionate frenzy of devotion and remorse
takes poison and dies —forgiven. Mrs. Bernard
Beere as the Countess Fedora Romanzoff achieved
a genuinely artistic success. Her emotional acting
was fine indeed, and her costumes were the despair
and admiration of a glittering d.c. The Jean de
Sirieux of M. Marius was a notable piece of work,
and rehabilitates that capable actor in the esteem
of his Sydney admirers. The Countess Olga
Soukereff of Miss Isabella Urquhart was a bright,
well-dressed, and altogether vivacious, impersona-
tion, which was highly appreciated by a discrimi-
nating but none too-well-filled house. Greteh,
the Russian police-agent of Mr. Kemp, was
lacking in spirit, and his make-up was that
of a shop-walker in a drapery emporium. Miss
Henrietta Watson was a very good Dmitri, and
several other actors and actresses played several
minor parts in a satisfactory manner. The Count
Loris Ipanoff of Mr. Otho Stuart was, however,
reminiscent of a Scotch clergyman of the English
Established Church doing duty in an aristocratic
London suburb, rather than of a Russian noble-
man of alleged Nihilistic antecedents. Neverthe-
less, his acting was very earnest, and well up to
the calibre of Mr. George Gordon’s scenery.
#* * »
At Sydney Criterion—which, by the way,
has entered on the last week of its lease by Messrs.
Brough and Boueicault—W. S. Gilbert’s three-act
farcical comedy, “Engaged,” has been running
since the Bth inst. to fairly good houses. This
piece, like most of Gilbert’s sallies into the realm
of stage-craft, belongs to the puissant empire of
topsyturvydom. It is mainly concerned with the
adventures of a young man who loves every
woman he meets, and makes a point of proposing
marriage to every pretty girl from the princess in
the palace to the maid iu the kitchen, or the girl
behind the bar, against whom he chances to
stumble. As a foil to his vagaries there are two
male characters, one, Belvawney, who receives a
thousand a year for preventing his double-
blessedness ; and another, Symperson, who is
to receive an income of a thousand a
year from the day the Lothario above-
mentioned settles down in the hymeneal
hash-bin. There is also a canny Scot who makes
an honest living by throwing trains off the line,
and running a private still under the most
religious auspices. Most of the piece belongs to
Mr. Titheradge, who, under the play-bill name of
Cheviot Hill, wanders about the earth seeking for
the damsel in whom he recognises the tree upon
which the apple of his heart is growing, and lie
finds her mostly all the time, and in every con-
ceivable situation. Mr. Boucicault, in a roman-
tically artistic make-up, did good work in the part
of Belvawney, and the Symperson of Mr. Law-
rence was a creditable performance, albeit perhaps
a trifle elephantine. Mr. Percy Lyndal, as the
braw Scot, hadn’t a good grip of the gait of the
Scottish tongue, and made several fearful lapses
into drawing-room English. Miss Romer was
more than admirable as the Caledonian dame, and
Miss Brenda Gibson was as winsome a lowland
lassie as ever spoke the Hielau’ dialect. Miss
Temple, as the gushing and romantic Belinda,
and Miss Pattie Browne, as the practical and
level-headed Minnie, held up their two ends of an
impossible _ plot with great Mat and to the
riotous delight of an enraptured audience. To-
night (Thursday) the “ Pickpocket ” will be
staged, and to-morrow night- (Friday) the
Brough and Boucicault Comedy Company will
make their final bow to a Sydney audience at
the Criterion Theatre, in a revival of Wycherley’s
old English comedy of “ The Country Girl.”
** * *
At Sydney Her Majesty’s, “Little Jack
Sheppard,” otherwise Billee Barlow, clanked his
chains for the final time on Friday last, which was
made a glorious benefit night of for the especial
profit and delectation of the charming little lady
who has lately lent her name to the burlesques
and extravaganzas produced at this popular place
of amusement. On Saturday night the alleged
something-or-other yclept “Randolph the
Reckless ” was produced before a crowded house,
which manifested many signs of disapproval
and disappointment before the piece got finally
through. Of Miss Billee Barlow herself it is im-
possible to speak too highly. She sings capitally,
dances well, and acts with vim and spirit; but we
are sorry to see so much talent wasted on a plot-
less, dialogueless, lifeless and generally meaning-
less dramatic wreck such as the piece in question.
The heroine, Miss Edith Vane, indulges in too
much ballad, and Miss L. Davis indulges in too
much dance. Moreover, this last-named lady
o’ersteps somewhat the modesty of nature, and in
her skirt-dances indulges a little too freely in the
calisthenic exercises of the champion high-kicker.
The villain of the piece, Mr. Geo. Walton, is meri-
toriously funny. Mr. Shine maintains liis reputation
as well as his brilliant name, and Mr. Barnes
ought to be promptly and thoroughly suppressed.
One of the most encouraging spasms in “Ran-
dolph ” is the scene of the duel, a /a “Corsican
Brothers,” between Mr. Shine and Mr. Walton,
and the latter makes some good business out of a
hysterical champagne bottle. Miss Billee Barlow,
whom everybody will be grieved to lose, will be
succeeded, on Saturday next, by “La Cigale,” <s
presented by Williamson’s Opera Troupe.
