** * *
At Coogee Aquarium money is still taken
at the front door, and the Bondi resort of the
same description has added a fighting kangaroo to
the monsters of the deep. At the Garrick Theatre
on Sunday night the Albus, who have been
starring the provinces during the week, dispensed
sweet music at collection rates, and the Fiske
Jubilee Singers have taken possession of the Cen-
tenary Hall, at which gruesome tabernacle
double-A-flat vocalism is nightly retailed at cur-
rent prices, and a lady-tenor warbles and jodels in
a style calculated to bring the blessings of harmony
within the reach of the slenderest purse.
** * *
The dread of a taxations Budget and a
coming rise in the price of meat seems to settle
more and more on Melbourne’s chest. The loud,
large voice of the showman, calling upon an ex-
hausted public to book their seats six days in
advance, is treated with cynical indifference.
Times are so bad that a change of bill makes no
difference worth mentioning, because one enter-
tainment is the same as another to those who stop
away. “ Faust up to Date,” at Melbourne Opera
House, has traded not unsuccessfully upon the
belief that a Gaiety burlesque is good for depres-
sion, but even the subtle humour of a Devil in a
red stove-pipe hat is losing its charm, Lonnen
gives them “ Ballyhooly ” in response to the
“particular desire” of somebody (the manager,
perhaps), without dispelling the gloomy suspicion
that a comic song grows serious in its old age,
and custom begins to stale the frenzy of “ Boom-
de-ay.” For Saturday, another burlesque, the
second of the series, is promised. This will be
produced under the title of “Joan of Arc,” and
rumour states that the Bogey Man will be resur-
rected in the course of the evening.
% sfc sfe *
Audiences great only in enthusiasm rolled
up to the two concerts and one performance of
“The Barber, ’at Melbourne Princess’s. “ Rigo-
letto,” on Saturday, was another genuine artistic
success which caused the vacant dress-circle seats
to look like indignant protests against the hollow-
ness of a so-called “ musical world.” Other parts
of the house were well attended, however, and the
shouts showered upon this fine performance
encouraged the hope that its repetition on Mon-
day and Tuesday would be duly appreciated. On
Wednesday, the Italians were to rest whilst an
English company, including Miss Colborne Baber,
Ida Osborne, Henry Stookwell, and Allan Morris
kept the ball rolling with “ Maritana.” To-night
(Thursday) the bill is “ Lucia,” to-morrow
“Maritana ” chips in again, and on Saturday the
Bride of Lammermoor may once more break the
heart of her mash in top boots.
* * *
Foli and Orlando Harley have been en-
gaged for the four remaining concerts, two each
week probably, and the Italian Opera season will
evidently be worked up to a gorgeous climax.
** * *
“ New Lamps for Old ” has not flourished
exceedingly at the Bijou. Some of its best jokes
pass almost unnoticed by the audience, and a good
many more jokes have escaped the eye of the com-
pany. Cecil Ward, in particular, takes no trouble
to play his part for all it is worth, whereas Cannam
errs in the opposite direction. Mr. and Mrs.
Brough and Cambourne show a decided improve-
ment since the opening night, and “New Lamps
for Old ” would have hummed in the humming
times of yore, anyway. On Saturday they put up
“ The Arabian Nights ” to fill in a week, after
which the amalgamated B. and B. forces will
attack “The Country Girl ” on a scale of unusual
splendour.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal the indomitable Bland
Holt continues to change his expensive spectacles
every Saturday without altering the fact that he
can t fetch a full house on the other five nights.
The supporters of British melodrama come for-
ward in their thousands and cheer the first pro-
duction of a show with deceptive gusto, and then
the supply of multitude is turned down low at the
meter. “ London Day by Day ” is a large, im-
posing thing of its class, exceedingly well played
in all directions, but it will retire on Saturday in
favour of sometlTing else, wherefore an honourable
mention of Charles Glenny, Baker, Walter Howe,
Albert Norman, Bland Holt, Miss Kate Bishop,
Edith Blande, Flora Anstead, and Harrie Ireland,
who shine brightest amidst a long string of per-
formers, will meet the requirements of the
case. Miss Harrie Ireland, seen in Mel-
bourne for the first time, made a good
impression when her nervousness wore off, and
is a very evident acquisition to the_ company.
The policeman who stalks on at the finish of these
splendid outrages and puts matters straight
doesn’t seem to satisfy the gallery any more, how-
ever. He has become an object of scorn by reason
of his constant readiness to arrest the villain with-
out a warrant, on the evidence of a casual gutter-
snipe and the unsupported testimony of a female
in distress. As an instrument of dramatic justice
the policeman is now quite obsolete, and his
appearance on the scene is the signal for the gods
to disperse with a loud noise which drowns the
voice of the hero as he explains that the clouds
have dispersed, or thanks heaven that somebody s
innocence is proved.
*m * *
The Dam piers breathed their last at the
Alexandra on Saturday, and are about to open a
provincial tour at Ballarat. Meanwhile the place
where Lily has brought down so many curtains on
tableaux in which she and a revolver were leading
features is being cleaned up in readiness for Hany
Rickards.
Concerts at the Exhibition and at the
Rotunda underneath the Siege of Paris are fairly
attended, and the Cogills, who have Slade
Murray and young Jules Simonsen singing for
them, seem to be thriving at St. George’s Hall.
The circus advertises eight performances a week,
as usual, and comes out with startling announce-
ments at regular intervals. Also, Little Lilian,
the Waxworks shootist, knocks an apple off the
head of a dog with a certainty which reflects
great credit on Lilian, and speaks volumes for the
calm trustfulness of the dawg.
** * *
Some of the dramatic scribes are surprised
that Walter Bentley makes Hamlet’s madness a
pretence. He couldn’t well do otherwise, unless
he were as ignorant of the text as some of his
critics. “As I, perchance, hereafter shall think
meet to put an an tick disposition on. ”
** # *
The Rignold season, at Adelaide Royal,
closed on Saturday (June 11), and despite the fact
that both brothers have been on the spot and that
a gift distribution was a feature in one perform-
ance, the venture has not been a startling success.
“Faust” did moderately well, but “In the
Ranks” and “Nowadays” failed to fetch folk
along. Signor Foli is now in musical possession
at the Royal, while his rivals for public favour are
the Liliputian Opera singers at Garner’s, and
Tommy Hudson at the Town Hall. Sheridan and
his Widow are at Broken Hill.
THE NEW STAR, OR SEVERE
COMPETITION.

The bad times with “ burlesque,” or the farce
that’s called “ screaming,”
When the Waxworks put forth such “ a powerful
cast ”
As the post - mortem head of the late Mr,
Deeming?
H.C.C.
-* * *
Williamson’s Opera Company finished up
their last week at Brisbane Opera House with
“Marjorie,” “Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay,” and a benefit
for Bracy on the last night. On Saturday morn-
ing at 8 a.m. they stole silently down the river
enveloped in a cold, grey mist. Rev. H. Rickards
commenced a mission at the Gaiety on Saturday
night and lifted up his voice in several hosannas
of the “ Knocked ’em in the Old Kent Road ”
variety. But the dark spectre of discontent looms
largely on the horizon, and the singing evangelist
aforementioned will scarcely fill the aching void
caused by the departure of Ryley and the giddy
chorus maiden. The Taylor-Carrington Co. is
bound North. The Royal re-opens on Saturday
(18th) with “The Vagabond,” for which a com-
pany engineered by one Hayman will be morally
responsible.
** * *
In the Melbourne Divorce-court, last
Monday, Miss Lily Dampier was “ put asunder”
from her' husband," Bill Watkin, costs to be paid
by the said Watkin if he can be found, which
seems an eminently improbable circumstance. It
s a short and simple story, the tale of Lily’s
married life. Mr. Watkin is an actor—we remem-
ber seeing him on the stage with a cocked-hat on
his head, the side of the cocked-hat being turned
to the front. He was playing, if we remember
rightly, with a nigger who afterwards got into
trouble or something, and with Alf. Boothman and
various others. There was a baleful look in
Boothman’s eye, as if he didn’t like Watkin—if
that ivas Watkin, for we are only speaking from
memory. Anyhow, Lily fell in love with him,
and they were secretly married in 1889.
For some unexplained reason they didn’t
tell the old man —we mean dear old
Alfred Dampier—till two years afterwards.
This proceeding doesn’t harmonise well with the
gentle, trustful look in Miss Dampier’s big, dark
eyes, but probably Watkin was responsible. Any-
how, in June last year that base deceiver fled
with another girl and hasn’t been seen since. We
don’t know the other girl, but we will bet she was
ugly; there is a demon of cussedness about the
bad man of the period, and when he leaves his
young and beautiful wife he generally elopes with
a painted harridan of forty. Miss Dampier looked
pretty and resigned in court, and wore a becoming
costume, and the divorce was granted on account
of “ misconduct ’’—with costs, as already men-
tioned, if anyone can find the defendant and
collect them.
At Poverty Point.
Do you know who is the lessee of the new and splendid
Sydney Lyceum, to be opened on Boxing Night ? Now,
guess.
Dear Bulletin,— Your remark re the Silbons’ recent
misfortunes, calls to mind the tact that I saw them in
Auckland, en route to Australia with Sells Bros., when a
friend of mine, a U.S.S. Co.’s purser who had travelled
with them previously, and did not know of their recent
trouble, delicately asked the reason of their mourning
<*arb. On being informed he expressed his sympathy,
when the married sister exclaimed : “ Yes, Mr. A., it was
a pity, it has quite spoilt the .principal act of our show."
This is bed-rock truth.— Jonah.
George Rignold took his Adelaide audience into his
confidence the other night. He said he was not a good
manager or he would have staged “ Henry V.” and
“ Romany Rye” instead of “My Partner” and “ In the
Ranks ” still he was glad that they liked him as the
Devil, but he was not as good as brother Bill. Cheers.
Rev. J. Reid, the new Melbourne Sunday lecturer, was
partly pulpit-exiled because of a really slashing sermon he
delivered at St. Augustine’s Congregational Church,
Hawthorn, a few months ago. Incautiously they let him
in the box on a Sunday night, and he forthwith pitched
into the rich warehousemen for keeping their clerks over-
time and giving them sixpence for a tea at De Freitas
the worthy eight o’clock rush man.
Rubinstein’s unactable opera, “ The Tower of Babel,”
is remarkable for the grandeur of its stage directions.
The final scene is laid in three horizontal compartments.
The top flat, says the inspired composer, must represent
Heaven and the Heavenly Host, the middle is reserved
for the Earth and the inhabitants thereof, and the bottom
floor is Hell, with Satan seated on his throne, surrounded
bv the infernal deities. Even Vincent Crummies would
have shrunk from producing this opera, on a. scale of
unusual magnificence for a short season. Initial difficulty:
Who would represent the Almighty ? George Rignold .

The Fitzgeralds are getting up steam for the production
of a lurid equestrian drama on the subject of Ned Kelly
and Company. Lance Lenton has prepared a series of
wild incidents and striking situations founded upon
everything the Kellys ever did and a number of things
they never thought of doing. Dead policemen are
scattered liberally amidst the plot, and Miss Kate Kelly
figures as the virtuous heroine who offers up prayers for
the gang whilst they are rampaging round the circus in
quest of blood and treasure.
Jean de Reszke is engaged to the daughter of a French
general whom he met in New York.
Mr. Herbert Flemming “had many offers from London
managers, but elected to play with the lady with whom
he has been associated in so many successes in Australia
and India.”
Liszt, when last in London, refused £lOOO for a single
concert. Paderewski netted over £lOOO for his two St.
James’ Hall recitals. Rubenstein’s last London concert
put £B5O into his pocket, and little Joseph Hofman
received for one recital over £5OO. About 100 years ago
Mozart gave a concert in Cornhill and netted £5.
At Hangman Berry’s lecture in London : “ Mr. Berry
simply rose, coughed, shuffled his feet, and tried to read
from half-a-dozen long sheets of paper fastened together
with a nursery-pin.”

Lady Violet Greville’s play, “Nadia,” matineed in
London, opens with a scene where a lady, walking in a
forest, is met by three officers of the Russian Imperial
Guard, and ravished. The chief criminal turns out to be
her own son whom she has not seen for years. Nice,
isn’t it ?

Lawrence, G. V. Brooke’s biographer, has started on a
life of Barry Sullivan. He was a strict teetotaller for

then they say of Sullivan, as of Richard Heales, that grog
would have kept him alive.

Quite everyone in Australia does not know that Fitz-
gerald Bros., our circus Barnums, are N.S.W. natives.
Bob Brough sighs over the craving for farcical comedy,
and would like to try his Sir Peter Teazle again.
There are no theatrical advertisements in the Paris
papers, but brief notifications are inserted as news.
Criticism is absolutely untrammelled by any advertising
contingencies, wherefore you do not see the canvasser and
his teeming family deadheads in the dress-circle, while the
bona-fide journalist pays to the pit.
Lambescasse, a Thespian character drawn in a Paris
paper, is familiar in Australia. He is a super., and ex-
plains how he has been conspired out of dramatic star-
hood. “Sir! I had just one chance, a scene with
Lemaitre. I killed him dead! He turned ashy pale
under his make-up. After that I was a marked man.”
Then Lambescasse, made up as a black slave, goes on the
stage to be kicked by the star.
Two Melbourne theatres are on the sick list, or about to
be. Dampier’s lease of the Alexandra has run out, and
the big house is not likely to find another tenacious
tenant yet awhile. The Royal is advertised “to be let”
at the conclusion of Bland Holt’s season. Probably the
three surviving managements will have the theatrical
market “proper” to themselves, with only occasional
bursts of opposition, for the next six months.
Foli, at the Princess’s, as the leading feature in a
Festival Concert, ought to mean a crowded house. At
the Town Hall he had a crowd only on his last night.
Frank Cates is net yet laid up in the Alfred Hospital,
Melbourne, nor has he undergone his operation. There is
a bed engaged for Franky at the Hospital, but its present
occupant is a tedious, undecided sort of person, who
neither gets well nor dies, but simply goes on airing the
bed for his successor. Last week Cates was still at large,
protesting against the aggravating conduct of “ this
other Johnnie,” and ready to be operated upon when sent
for. P.S.—lt was on Cates’ own authority that we said
he was in Alfred Hospital.
Critic Prancisque Sarcey on Coquelin : “ Ilis snub
nose and wide-split mouth do not accord with this part.”
Such is Sarcey’s fashion, and it is relished, but imagine
Australian critics thus varying the “creditable” and
“ excellent rendition.”
The Bungaree Man came to Melbourne last week for his
first play and picked Bland’s “ London Day by Day,” but
got back to his lodgings by nine o’clock. Landlady:
“Very short play, isn’t it?” Bungaree: “Yes, but I
stopped till the curtain went down.” .

In Maoriland, three generations of one family in the
female line are on the boards : Mrs. Walter Hill, her
daughter, Mrs. Kennedy, and her grand-daughter, little
Ruby Kennedy.
James Kennedy, the “ human ostrich,” is dying in New
York. This ungainly fowl is a person who, for eight
years has eaten nails, tacks, crockery, stoves and broken
tumblers in a penny gaff for the joy and edification of the
public.
A Melbourne paper asserts that one of the big Sydney
hotels is run on such desperately religious principles,
that the manager wouldn’t take in Sara Bernhardt
“ because she was an actress,” and also declined to admit
Johnny Toole.

A Toowoomba girl—a great pal of vice-regality—loudly
announced in the dress-circle of Brisbane Opera House,
the other night, that she and some of her friends had
been trying the “ Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay ” and had managed
the high-kick pretty well. “ Hbw far did you get?”
enquired a male admirer. “Oh, about as high as your
shoulder,” replied the gentle maid of high degree. No
wonder Lady Norman seeks relief from thfs sort of thing
in the committee work of the Lady Bowen lying-in
hospital.

The Italians make their most glaring transitions from
the sublime to the ridiculous in “ Faust." When the
Devil has brought down the house by bis display of fear
and trembling at the sight of the cross-handled swords he
bows his way to the wings and fetches Valentine. Then
the gentleman in scarlet shakes the hand of Marguerite s
brother as they both bow together. There seems to be
more good fellowship than ever amongst the Italians since
Williamson took them. They feel all the happier for
getting bigger salaries, perhaps.
The fully-qualified lady doctors in Great
Britain now number 1,200.
There are now in N.S.W, only 4458 full-
blooded aborigines and 3015 half-castes —a decrease
of 243 for the year.
Ford and Greene, the two Vic. Railway
Commissioners under Speight, will not do badly
by being suspended. They pocket 18 months’
salary in a lump and will presently re-enter the
Public Service by other doors.

Australian address for Is. 3d. in stamps. Posted
to any English address for Is. lOd. in stamps.
Liquidator Creer finds that the liability
of the shareholders of Abigail’s bank—cradled in
usury—is unlimited. “ I have made some investi-
gation into the books of the company, and I have
found that advances have been made to 17 persons
—officers or directors of the company—to the
extent of over .£60,000, and on security not worth
looking at. There are other details in the accounts
which also show a very unfortunate state of
things.”
Griffith had a warm time with an unem-
ployed deputation last week. “We want bread,’’
said one, “and what’s more we mean to have it.”
Dancing around his tabic Sam spluttered out:
“ You mustn’t talk like that here, you know.” It
was lovely whilst it lasted. They roasted him
fairly, and told him plainly that they were only
trying to live up to his own teaching, and threw
“ Wealth and Want ” in his face to the utter dis-
comfiture of the gingerbread statesman.
The disappearance of financier Fogarty
recalls the quiet method adopted .by a Sydney
attorney’s accountant who made tracks a few
years ago. An audit of his books showed a deficit
of £2OOO. He took into his confidence a cabman
whom he had frequently employed, and who
agreed to conceal him in his house for six months.
Meanwhile he grew a big beard, and, when every-
thing was ready, turned his notes into gold, went
to Newcastle, and got away in a coal-ship to South
America.
Last week the New Oriental Bank closed
peacefully and went where the good banks go.
It was built on the ruins of the old Oriental Bank,
which also collapsed some few years ago, so the
name is evidently an unlucky one. The cable-
grammer got into a more than usually irrespon-
sible condition over this incident, and announced
that the collapse would create renewed uneasiness
in England about Australian financial institutions
—which is a remarkable statement, seeing that
the Oriental Bank was itself English, and it is
Australia which ought to feel the uneasiness.
Also, one of the Foreign-trade papers of Sydney
rose at full moon to point out that the smash was
brought about by the malign influences of Pro-
tection, because the defunct institution had
,£300,000 invested in Melbourne, on solid security,
out of over £7, 000,000 of assorted liabilities.
Later on it turned out that the fall was brought
about by the rush of Scotch depositors, so the
collapse was probably owing to Ereetrade, but
that paper has dropped the subject now.
For some hidden reason which will never
be explained till the waste-paper basket gives up
its dead, Sydney Truth allows “ a correspondent ”
to use its columns as a “meejum ” for the follow-
ing. The person alluded to is that common-
place and bumptious person, J. D. Young,
M.L.C., and the thing is evidently m&Sfi for
blank verse :
“Shout!” You bet. The Bulletin has one
question to ask just here —will J. D. Young state
definitely that he didn’t write this himself ? The
thing is so exceedingly like what J. D. Young
obviously thinks of himself, and the blanky verse
is so very like the sort of blank verse that J. IX
Young would produce, judging from the things
we have heard him “ hurl ” at municipal officials
upon whom he was “jumping” in all the pride
and glory of his stupendous power, that it looks
suspicious. Still, in a way, the statements are
true. Mr. Young grabbed the offices of municipal
councillor and transit commissioner, and he
scorned all the rest—probably because he couldn’t
get’em. He is “ fearless in denunciation ” right
enough—anyone who has heard him bang the
table and has listened to his shriek and howl will
endorse this. He does “ hurl defiance ” at nearly
everybody—we seem to hear a hoarse voice
saying “ Gerrout !’* and the voice sounds
very like Young’s. He is “blunt in speech”
beyond the slightest question, for, very often,
he simply asks the other man to “come
outside ” and then sits down. He was in Parlia-
ment for a year or so, and then he was bounced
out, and couldn’t get himself re-elected for West
Sydney—in fact, he never wilL be re-elected.
Leastways we hope not, as we don’t want to see
Parliament made into a heaven for uncultured
rum-sellers. Also, he kept a public-house, and
the other day he was tendered a banquet, at which
every important guest was named Mick—except
one who was named Crick.
He stands—a monument of integrity
Scorning office and bribes —
Ever the friend of the down-trodden poor.
Whose word is his bond—
Respected by all. Fearless
In denunciation of wrong ;
Hurling defiance at cliques and parasites ;
Carefully guards the rights of citizens ;
Just, alike to friend and foe ;
No honeyed words—blunt and pointed in speech—
Seeks not to give offence. Undeviating
From the path of rectitude, whose memory
Will live when others have been long forgot.
With reverence we greet and shout:
“ Long life to J. D. Young,”

Speaker. The Ministry claim 28 supporters, so
there is evidently a discrepancy somewhere. The
present is the fifth no-confidence motion in 12
months, and the Government still survives, though
in a shaky condition.

THE VOICE OF THE SLAVE.
Australia awaken t O troubled !' O dreaming
Of pleasure, O lover !' O sweet, and 0 brave.
J ue-pleasure is false, the lover is seeming •
1 fie- offer of joy is the voice of the slave.
Australia awaken ! the breathing grows louder,.
I he dream is a dance, O breaker ! O wave 1
O glory 1 O triumph ! the-heart it grows prouder,
ine dance is a dream in the grasp of a slave.
Australia awaken ! the sighing is deeper,
Ihu struggle a horror ;■ the brink of a grave '■
Ihe hideous dream that troubles the sleeper
Is- the-poisonous thrill of the touch of the slave;-
Australia, awaken !' O lover! O beauty ■
I he nightmare is passing ;■ a vision, O knave.
Awaking ! awaking ! to virtue and duty
o pinning with loathing the kiss of the slave.

“ Sir Henry Parkes,” according to the
most recent and neatest of his many interviewers,
“ in his walks wears an old felt hat of enormous
size, with a long peacock’s feather stuck in the
band.” The writer goes on to say:—“David
Blair tells an anecdote relating to the size of Sir
Henry’s head. Forty years ago Blair, Parkes,
and Deniehy were spending an evening together
in Sydney. Blair picked up Parkes’ hat
and put it on. It covered him to the
shoulders, and what Parkes’ hat did to
Blair, Blair’s did to The old
man still keeps up this game. For instance,
over a year ago, while so lame that lie had
almost to be carried into tine hall, he attended a
political meeting aft Paddington which was being
addressed by “Jack” Want. Jack, who has a
big head, as heads go, had left his hat on the table
at which old man Parkes sat, and while the other
was speaking Sir Henry calmly took up the tile
and poised it on one bump of bis own colossal
skull to the great delight of the audience —and the
great discomfiture of the noteasily-abasbed Q.-.C.
The only paper in Adelaide not conducted
on Union lines is, it is alleged, a religious sheet
which employed until recently woman labor, and
pays cheap Chinese wages to employes.
Attorney Yon Doussa, at Mount Barker
(S.A.), was cross-examining a fellow German in a
complicated assault ease in which both the parties
were Teutons. “ Ah, Mr. Jaensch,” he said, as
the continued to baffle him, “ it is easy to
gee that the plaintiff is related to you.’ ‘ YaJ
was the replv, “der blaintiff vas mein bruder-m-
--law.” A smile,of triumph passed over the sohei-
tQrls face as be exclaimed sententiously, I
thought so !” “ Yell, dat is notinks,' added the
witness, evidently resenting the emphasis placed
upon the comment, “ (ler defendant vas mein
.cousin.” “ Oh, I daresay,” said the lawyer know-
ingly, “but you think more of the brother-in-law
than the cousin !” The witness put first his right
and then his left fore-finger between bis teeth and
'afterwards.said with an air which carried convic-
tion tp .all who it —“ Look here, Von
Doussa, of I jaite dis finger or of I bite dat finger,
it hurt shust der same !”
Dear Bulletin, — You had a par recently
, chronicling the physical breakdown of Sir Harry
Atkinson, ex-Premier of Maoriland. Those who
remember the “fighting major” as I remember
him, seven or eight years ago, would be surprised
to bear it. Atkinson, with his rough-hewn face,
shaggy Brows, over-hanging eyes of piercing
Brightness, his stalwart, though somewhat un-
gainly form, his strident, combative voice, and bis
tireless energy, might have been taken as a very
model of middle-aged virility. It was the man’s
very physical tirelessness, more than his un-
doubted ability, that made him, for nearly 20
years, the most prominent figure in M.L. politics.
Grey is a grand old man, ho doubt, but he
coddles himself and saves his strength for an
occasional effort. Atkinson has never spared
himself—he led his party, he whipped for his
' party—in fact, he was his party."" It was Atkm-
-1 son first, all the rest nowhere.' Mitchelson, Dick,
Bryce, and Kolleston, the ablest 6f bis many col-
leagues in many Government's, were mere min-
nows beside the Triton. I' doubt not that the
insane practice of pushing all the parliamentaiy
business of a country like Maoriland into one
short session of 12 or 13 weeks hits had much to do
with the failing of Atkinson’s health. Fancy,
committee-work from 10 to 12.30. House from
2.30 p.m. till the same hour or later the following
morning, and that routine five <1 a week!
’Twas Atkinson who made the parliamentary
Juggernaut move on at this fatal speed, IMany it
crushed, and now even his own fine constitu-
tion is'a sacrifice.—Yours, Timi Piripi.

IN MEMORIAM —“AUGUR.”
(EDWARD SAMUEL CHAPMAN.)
“ Cold earth ” is not his resting-place
Who paid “ the Turf ” an ardent court
And strove to make it honest sport—
It folds him in a warm embrace.
A happy union ! Cypress wreath
We cannot lay on “ Augur’s ” grave,
Let spotless “Orange Blossom ” wave,
His work was pure who sleeps beneath.
And when the Spring comes round again,
And all the South is racing-mad,
The thought will rise—Our Sport has had
No truer friend than “ Augur’s pen.
H.C.C.

Society
Art additional china is now, it would
appear, being lent to Sydney harbour-steamer
collisions by the “captain of our boat” getting on
to “ the other boat ” as soon as the smash comes —
“presumably for the purpose o’ helping our
people on to the other boat.” Then the boats
drift apart, and by-aml-byo “our boat,” not
having sunk, “our captain” conies back from
“ the other steamer ” in a ro w-boat and resumes
his post.
=£ # #
Some people in this world arc enterprising
enough to jump upon a car that has just been
wrecked by collision with another of its kind, and
lie up for a week or two with “ severe shock ”
prior to an action for damages agiinstthe com-
pany.
# # #
It is whispered that the Elder “exploring ”
expedition came to grief because the relatives of
the bachelor knight objected to his wasting their
money in what looked like a bad speculation.
Left to himself Sir Thomas would have sent Lind-
say out again.
* # *
Curious things may happen to a bride in
Australia. About 10 months back in England the
lovely daughter of poor but haughty parents
married a man in business much to the “ family’s ”
horror. The business person had lucre, of course,
and he brought his bride to Australia on a pro
tracted honeymoon, hoping that his exalted but
dead-broke parents-in-law would appreciate the
advantages of the match when the baker and the
batcher began to talk bailiff. The young couple
put up at a fashionable Melbourne hotel, but they
left again with much suddenness. The boots at
the hotel happened to be a disreputable brother of
the bride’s. He had disappeared from ’Ome after
serving a term for smashing a man’s skull over a
game of cards.
# # #
The Sydney woman-sulfragists have been
trotting out once more that hoary story about the
smaller percentage of female criminals than of
male. What becomes of the notion if we deduct
from the masculine record the crimes of violence
which women can’t commit, and the commercial
offences for which they don’t get the chance ?
# * •
Lord and Lady Hopetoun are not in
especial favour with the Melbourne Liedertafels
because they fidget and yawn fearfully at the
concerts, and sit up in the gallery instead of
making a glittering spectacle of themselves in the
front seats. The Lochs used to vice-regalise in
the place of honour and applaud the part songs
with great vigour, like the wily old soldiers that
they were. As for giddy deputy-Governor Robin-
son, well the Liedertafel fellows simply doated on
him, and serenaded him once or twice. If he were
to come back to Melbourne and die, they would
roll up to his funeral like one man. “Father, in
thy gracious keeping now we leave Sir William
sleeping,” sung by 500 voices would be most
impressive. Not that there is any fear of Robin-
son passing away yet awhile.
*“" * #
One of the suspended Australian “ financial
institutions,” was making frantic efforts to obtain
an extension of time from the duped depositor.
The usual additional audit was ordered, and
officials were hard at work giving the paper assets
some touch of reality. The Co.’s solicitor had, of
course, his own little overdraft—covered by a bill-
of-sale over some furniture. “ Make the schedule
look large,” said Satan’s son; so, with a great
flourish, an additional line was given for the fry-
ing-pan and another for the poker. The working
expenses of this very company are equal to 38 per
cent, of its capital. A long period of grace has
been granted, and yet the depositors expect to get
their money returned.
At the Governor’s levee on 24th May a
recently-elected member of Parliament was driven
up to Government House, Brisbane, by a Kanaka
coachman. This is what ThE Bulletin calls
“ rubbing it in.”
There is to be an interesting event at an
Australian vice regal residence very soon. Lady
Hopetoun is to be asked for the loan of her
perambulator.

At Montalbion, N.Q. . when Gov. Norman
went through, a “native” (white) stalked up :
“ Are you Mr. Norman, the Governor?” “Well,
yes.” “ Will you shake hands with a feller?”
They shake. “You’re not half a blanky sort of
bad blank to be a Governor, after all! ”
In the free and easy districts of Bananaland one
may live years with mates and only know
them as “ Blower Bill,” “Lanky Joe,” “ Yorky,”
“Bandy Peter,” and so on. Hence, let not
the following be deemed a romance. Governor
Norman was at Herberton, and Herberton’s
Mayor, himself officially admitted within the
circle of vicc.-regal champagne-corks, produced a
friend. “ Yer Exe’llency, permit me to introduce
to you one of our pioneers, known to all the dis-
trict as a thorough white man, my old friend Mr.
—c*r—er—my old friend—er—Mup Sandy ! ” At a
small town on the Oloncurry, the leading man
didn’t worry at all, but simply brought his friends
up one after the other— “Yer Ex’leuoy, Joe
Bango ; yer Ex’lency, Bucking Bob ; yer Ex’leucy,
BarC ooted Peter &c., &c. And his Ex. said he
rather preferred it that way.

The principal streets of the capital city of
Western Australia are kept clean by one man,one
broom, and a wheelbarrow.

Dear Bulletin*- Civilisation progresses in
the West. My friend the chemist having absented
himself for a game of billiards, I was rather un-
willingly left in charge of the shop, a short time
ago, when in walked a western crazier and asked
in a remarkably hesitating and timid way : “ Do
you sell any tooth-brushes?” “Oh, yes; what
sort would you like ? Soft ones, hard ones, or
here are very good electric ones, or “Oh,
pick me a good one yourself I ” At once I
“ dropped ” to the fact that my customer was a
better judge of merinoes than of tooth-brushes, and,
selecting the oldest instrument in stock, I care-
fully handled it so as not to shake out any bristles,
when my customer said with continued hesitation *
“ Got any tooth-powder?” “ Oh, yes ; which do
you prefer? There is pearl-ash, and carbolic
powder, and camphorated chalk and .” “ Oh,
pick mo a good brand yourself.” This was my
chance at last to dispose of the big, old jar of
Japanese tooth-powder, the ugliness of which had
been an eyesore to me for a long time, and which
I knew the chemist had unsuccessfully tried to sell
for 10s. right through all the years of the silver-
boom. I had no trouble to persuade my man that
this, though costly, was the only article a gentle-
man could use, and I was just starting to make a

A Sydney chemist’s trusted assistant who
took advantage of his position to perform illegal
operations without the knowledge of his employer,
made in three years something like <£-1000. There
are in Sydney numbers of medical practitioners,
chiefly unqualified and mostly steeped in ignor-
ance, who do practically nothing save procure
abortion, and who live in splendour and make big
fortunes. To absolutely catch thorn is almost an
impossibility, and yet the detectives can point
out to you half-a-score of grasping, merciless
ruffians, each of whom has on his fingers diamonds
as big as walnuts and on his callous soul tire life-
blood of a lot of despairing, helpless women.
* * #
Is Australia a democratic country ? When
Governor Jersey paid his recent visit to Bal-
ranald (N.S.W.) the other day and was invited to
take a black-handled knife and a fork with three
prongs at a banquet, the local srtobs had a troubled
time. Accordirrg to usage, tiro Mayor should
have presided, but that official is a blacksmith,
and the manly trade of horse-shoeing is low and
common, so he was ejected, by acclamation, and
the P.M. presided in his stead. Years ago, when
Governor Loftus made a progress trp country he
struck a town where tire Mayor was a barber, yet
apparently the people weren’t ashamed of him.
Anyhow, the shampoo man came out in full regalia
and welcomed His Ex., and then Lord Augustus,
being rather bristly about the chin, wentalong to his
little shop, and his worship shaved him, and half-
talked him to death, and washed his head, and cut
him on the chin; and after that he got into his
robes of State and presided at the banquet, and
made a neat speech; and the ex-ambassador got up
with the gash still on his lower jaw, and responded
cordially. No doubt the heavens ought to have
fallen, but they didn’t—in fact, they keep up to
tins day. The barber went into Parliament, and
if Loftus had only stayed a little longer in
N.S.W. they might have met again when the ex-
threepenny-sliave man came up with an "Address-
iu-Reply.”
Let us hope that when the Mayor of Balranalcl
comes to Sydney Governor Jersey will ostenta-
tiously invite him to dine at Government House;

A certain rather well-known Victorian is
now declared to have fled from the chance of
being wanted over the G. N. Taylor bank job.
Melbourne begins at last to understand why
Taylor was handled so gingerly by the Crown
Law Department.
The village settlements, instituted for the
relief of Melbourne distress and the “opening up ”
of Victorian country, will not want for music.
Most of the assisted families have gone forth into
the wilderness with a piano which they couldn’t
otherwise dispose of.

Marshall Lyle takes steps to recover
Deeming s papers and effects. They now say
F.B.D.’s deeds were not destroyed, but that they
bye after him, and are circulating around the
Victorian Ministry. The parson said Frederick’s
writings were wicked and salacious, so there is
probably much truth in the newest rumour.
# # #
Dr Springthorpe complains that whilst
men of science were warned off Deeming’s head
showmen were allowed to take casts of the
deceased murderer. But then the Waxworks has
a sort of vested interest in Chamber of Horrors
people, and the Government doesn’t prevent
Doctor S. from paying his shilling to see “The
Hands that did the Deed ” under a glass-case.
Phil. Stuart says that Deeming’s invaluable ser-
vices to the Melbourne Waxworks are fairly
crowned by “The Hands that did the Deed.”
They give the finishing touch to the agony.
Deeming cementing his victim in the empty room
—the Vampire laid out on trestles, from a cast
taken after death—a lurid lecture on Deeming,
illustrated by photographic views on a sheet —
finally, “ The Hands,” &c., including the Fatal
Fingers with Fine Filbert Nails. Science couldn’t
have made such valuable use of the gentleman,
anyway.

good sausages, too. All the bad sausages are
wrapped in the Sydney Daily Telegraph.

Dear Bulletin Yob are down heavily
on poetic wattle-blossom, and with reason. In
verse it has lost its beauty and long since dis*
sipated its odour. It needs a rest. But what
about the mopoke that “laughs” and “gurgles”
and the ’possum that runs about in the daylight ?
Why doesn’t somebody jump heavily on the brick-
and-mortar poet who makes the miserable moping
owl and the nocturnal phalangor do these things ?
The melancholy “pope-pope” of the former is
about as much like laughter as the nasal “quaw ”
of the wood-duck, and I don’t see why the latter
should be so invidiously neglected. Then again
there is the long, festive “whish” of the native
cat, and the vocal jocularity of the jolly native
bear. Why do tire poets leave them out in the
cold ? —Crosscut.
Evidently when Lord Glasgow accepted
work as Governor of Maoriland he didn’t know
what awaited him at Wellington. His aide wired
from Melbourne to have the beds aired, and the
Cabinet hadn’t the nerve to telegraph back that
there weren’t any beds, and that the house was
quite empty, so when K.E. arrives he will pro*
b.ibly sit down on the bare floor with a look of
considerable astonishment. The furniture was,
in former days, supplied at the expense of the
Treasury, bat owing to the fearful row which Gov.
Bowen made over a baby’s bassinette, or some*
thing of the sort, it was decided that each poten-
tate must lay in his own tables and chairs for the
future. Nobody explained this to Glasgow, how-
ever, and when lie gets there his wife will pro*
bably have to run out for some sausages, while the
Governor himself borrows a frying-pan from the
woman in the next house, and the aide knocks up
a fire and prepares a sort of scrambling feed on
the mantelpiece. As for sleeping accommodation
the Earl will either have to sit on a hat-box beside
the fire for the first night or else sleep the best way
he can in a corner. There ought to be some
tolerably strong language that day in the Glasgow
family—especially if the Governor is out in the
back yard looking for wood at two a.in. and falls
heavily over a root.

# « •
This is the way they make divorce-evidence
in Sydney. Man calls on a person whose matri*
monial relations are strained. “ Mr. Blank, I
believe?” “Yes.” “ Well, I’m going to start in
your line of business, and I’ve been recommended
to you as likely to give me some points.” “ Cer-
tainly. Come inside.” “Thanks —can’t stay just
now, but if you’ll join me in a couple of hours at
the Snake-in-the-Grass Hotel, we’ll have a liquor
and talk things over.” Blank keeps appointment,
is introduced to a “ friend,” has numerous drinks,
and is carted off to finish the evening at the
friend’s house in the suburbs. He is shown into
a room, the friends beg to be excused for a moment
and vanish, whereupon a dishevelled young person
glides in and flings herself upon Blank’s manly
bosom. Enter then three strangers, one of whom
loquitur —“ Your name’s John Blank? Mine’s
Slush, private detective; and these men are
witnesses that I find you on these excellent terms
with a woman in a house of ill-fame !” There was
no get-away from that. Mrs. Blank got her
divorce, and married the fellow who had arranged
the,whole job.

The severity of the sentences passed by
North Queensland Judges is attributed to the
extreme difficulty they have in securing convic*
tions. For instance, in Charters Towers not so
many years ago, the police laid for a suspected
beef-pickler and caught him red-handed. There
wasn’t a flaw in the evidence. Verdict—“not
guilty.” Someone asked a juryman why they
didn't return a guilty verdict.. “Well, it's this
way—he might be able to do as much for us one
of these days. D’ve see !”

An enterprising N.S.W. ex-prisoner lately
“ raised the wind ” by representing himself as one
of his late keepers, and calling sah rom on a
brother of a confines, who instantly swallowed
the bait in tho interests of his close-bound and
hominy-fed relative. Not satisfied with obtaining
a handsome consideration for his services as
medium, he improved tho occasion by further
borrowing his victim’s watch and chain upon some
plausible pretext. The quondam warder not turn-
ing up in due course, the sympathising brother
inquired at the gaol for Warder So-and-So, whoso
appearance “ flabbergasted ’’ tho simpleton. The
dupe sheepishly retired from the scene, sadder
and wiser.
* * *
Two well-dressed “ pals ” have been tour-
ing Sydney, “ taking down ” the hard-worked
’bus-drivers, who can ill-atford to be relieved of
their cash, as they are held responsible for every
penny, even for the bad Coin which is occasionally
palmed-off on them at night. The modus is to
mount an empty box-seat and while the first
arrival, who plants himself close up to the
“ whip,” absorbs the whole attention of Ids victim
by some interesting conversation, the other deftly
“ goes through ” his cash-box. The actual thief
was detected the other night in King-street, bub
dexterously “slipped his cable” by jumping clean
off the bus. Of course, his mate was highly
indignant at the wickedness of the operator, and
“ bluffed ” the driver by pretending to chase the
robber.

Dear Bulletin, —lf you wish to know why
the nice, smooth leather straps that are attached
to railway-carriage windows disappear so often
and so mysteriously, I can tell you : You can’t
beat them for razor-strops. No one “out back”
who shaves should be without one, and very few
are. Station huts bristle with them.— H. J. Mac,

The handsome daughter of one of the
superseded Victorian Railway Commissioners has
taken a billet, and is now an assistant in Mel-
bourne Mutual Store. The casual visitor may
identify her by the fact that she alone among the
lady employes is permitted to wear her bat, or
bonnet, while “on duty.”

Dear Bulletin,— A suggestion for the
N.S.W. unemployed. Coroners’ juries nowreceivo
fees. Let a group of men ballot for one of their
number to commit suicide, while the rest get the
billets thus created for jurymen. Keep on doing
this often enough, and the unemployed problem
is solved. --Starving.

for‘the push” when Robert gets roused. The
buckle-end means the hospital or graveyard.

The case of Kate Butler, recently tried on
a charge of bigamy, at Newcastle (N.S.W.),
revealed a curiously free-and-easy state of tilings
on the part of everybody concerned. The defen-
dant, at the age of 16, was married to a miner
named Croxall, and at the marriage a document
was handed in setting forth that tho girl was 18
years of age. that her father was dead, and that
her mother consented to the marriage. Croxall
swore that he took this paper to the mother, and
that she signed it, and the mother swore that she
didn’t sign it, and that she knew nothing about
the marriage. The father was not dead, and all
the parties to the transaction were aware of tho
fact, but none of them seemed to know who was
responsible for the statement to the contrary. The
pair lived together for three weeks, and then the
bride went home and never came back. The
bride’s father “had heard of the first marriage,”
but didn’t regard it as being legal, and tie and his
wife were both present at the second marriage.
The defendant acknowledged both marriages, but
swore that her first husband threatened to shoot
her, and she only married him to avoid being shot.
Then the Judge directed the jury to find a verdict
of guilty, and the jury rose up unanimously and
found a verdict of not guilty. Whereupon the
Crown Prosecutor asked the Judge to warn the
first husband that he wasn’t at liberty to marry
ao-ain, and the Judge declined to inteifeie. Also,
he remarked that lie had been “ insulted ” by the
jury and retired in a state of considerable indigna-
tion.

A big club “sacked” its cook at the in-
stance of a person of prominence. Reason: “He
keeps the knives so sharp that they cut a man’s
face in two while he is eating.”
# # *
Sydney Master Hairdressers’ Association
has rescinded the resolution limiting the hours
of the trade from 8 a.in. to 8 p.m. on live days of
the week and till 10 p.m. on Saturdays, and has
reverted to the original working hours, 8 a.m. till
9 p.m. and 10.30 p.m. on Saturdays. What ghastly
slavery !

Semines, of the Alabama, had a virulent
hatred of Vanderbilt. His ambition was to
destroy a Frisco mail and gold steamer, owned by
the Commodore, with which view Semmes had
the Alabama painted up and disguised as the U.S.
cruiser Tornado, which name was even painted on
] ie r bows. Thus he got up right close to tho mail,
but found she was going into port, with no gold,
of course, and 300 passengers. Reluctantly ho
abandoned his design, but gave the ship’s company
a terrific fright. He sent aboard his handsomest
lieutenant (Chapman), clad in a magnificent
uniform of purple and gilt. Chapman “frightened
them to rights,’in the saloon, and had the ladies
fainting about him in all directions, but when ho
told them they could go on, they snipped all the
gold off his uniform, and took all his feathers, for
mementoes. “By says Semmes, “you’ve
moul ed ! ”
Notwithstanding the coddling of the sugar
industry, the distress from want of employment
in Brisbane is becoming accentuated. It is
estimated that there are from 1500 to 2000 genuine
workers unemployed in and around Brisbane) and
this in a population of 70,000;

The story goes that Richard, on calling at the
railway-offices, tlie other day, for documents
necessary to his ease, was told that they were all
locked up and that Minister Wheeler had taken
the key up country. When, however, Symes
minion came round he was told to sit down and

perty of the department was placed at his disposal)

appreciable percentage of the commissariat;
Smokers arc divided into two classes.
There is the ordinary plain smoker whose sole
ambition is to take a certain amount of smoke into
Ids system out of a short, black pipe and blow it
out again—most Australians belong to this ctass -
and tlie fancy or ornamental smoker who takes in
a whitf and thin sends it forth via his ears, or.
doesn’t send it forth at all, but simply keeps it in
his system, or who takes in a long, satisfying puff
and then discharges it through Ids boots. There
is a Frenchman belonging to this class in Sydney,
and he is a fearsome man to know. You may
meet him in the evening when he isn’t smoking,
and converse with him, and he looks just like an
ordinary man. Tlien you can go to the theatre
with him, and .sit out a five-act Horror, and have
drinks between each act, and lie won’t manifest
the slightest desire to smoke all the time. And
when the five-act Horror is over you can go with
him and have an oyster supper, and after the
supper the chances are that he will lean back in
his chair, and slowly blow a long blue cloud out
through his teeth with an air of absolute un-
concern. It is the last whiff of tlie,cigar that he
finished before you met him some five hours previ-
ously, and after putting the drinks and the
oysters on top of it ho brings it out from under-
neath that load of provender as good as new.
He is quite harmless, but most people, when they
see him first, suspect him of spontaneous com-
bustion, or of having sold himself to the Devil.

such a tender interest when Duncan Gillies’
famous octopus bill was before Parliament. The
Fitzroy eockspur was the Speight Gillies fancy in

and Wheeler are quite of the other way of think-
ing, and so David Syme's property at Stud ley
Park is looking up. Strange! to say, the Age pre-
serves a solemn silence on the very subject that
was formerly of such vital interest; to Melbourne,
and the masterly policy of tho Government in
proposing a new line to “ retrench ” upon is not
brought under public notice.

Ned’s Delicate Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Ned knew I was short of tobacco one day,
And that I was too proud to ask for it;
He hated such pride, but Ins delicate way
Forbade him to take me to task for it.
I loathed to be cadging tobacco from Ned,
But, when I was just on the brink of it :
“ I’ve got a new brand of tobacco,” he said —
“Try a smoke, and let’s know what you think
of it.”
Bydney, May, 1892.
HENRY LAWSON.
S. A. possesses some 1500 or so Jaypees, or
one to every 213 persons.
N.S. VV. Government starts State irrigation
works in the Warren district by treating a block
of land 150 miles long by 100 broad.
M‘IlgrifF wants to establish a “Manasseh ’

The associatecl-foreign-trade-press cable-
man, who pulls the Anglo-Australian wires,
begins to snap out all the nasty tilings possible
about N.S.W. Premier Dibbs.
From London Weekly Bulletin :—
As showing the terrible deprivations our aristocracy arc
now' going through, I may say that Messrs. F. M. Prime
& Co., of Smithfleld Market, showed me one day last
week an entry of a basket of strawberries, bought for and
eaten by the Marquis of Lome (when at Bradford), the
cost per ounce being 4s., or of the basket £1 4s. I need
say no more. This is why the nation rots or dies, I say.
We pay this wretched specimen of the human race, or his
wife, £6,000 a year, so that he can buy strawberries at
£1 4s. a basket, whilst the miserable tax-payer dies in the
gutter or suicides wholesale ! And we profess to wonder
at dynamite! !

SPORTING NOTIONS.
That eloquent tough, Mr. Mitchell, expresses
his fixed opinion that the coining Sullivan-Cbrbett fight is
a gate-money swindle, heralding their joint appearance in
a new play.
# # *
Reported in American papers that an Aus-
tralian—name not mentioned—is going to swim down
Niagara Falls. Let’s hope it’s Salmon Wilson.
# # *
Native Maorilanders, whetlier white or coloured,
sweep all before them in the local tugs of war. The
champion and hitherto unbeaten Maori team weighs
lot stone.
* * *•
Warwick, the English rider, and Tom Busst,
Australia’s champion bicyclist, will probably ride a path-
mile in August. Crisp and Broadbent, the Victorians,
say they will accommodate Warwick with a mile on the
road.
# * *
A Victorian metallician is reported to have been
struck at Adelaide for the City and Cup double, the City,
Cup and Hurdle treble, the City, Cup, Hurdle and Steeple
quadruple—a thousand each way. Probably a lie.
* # *
A word with the N.S.W. Rowing Association.
If possible keep the crew together who rowed at Brisbane.
Let them have a row together if only once or twice a
month until time for steady training for next race. Under
these conditions N.S.W. may once more get to the front.
Under the present system of pitchforking eight men
into a boat a few weeks before the race they never will.
» * *
“ Jumbo ” Walker, who has been in terribly
had health since his typhoid attack, benefited to the
extent of £9O by a Sydney football match last week.
* • •
A Melbourne football benefit to the relatives of
the Mornington team (drowned in Hobson’s Bay), realised
nearly £l5O. One of the most pathetic incidents con-
nected with this terrible disaster, was that of the Rev.
Mr. Caldwell, whose three stalwart sons—their bodies are
unrecovered—perished with the team, reading the burial
service over the two bodies washed ashore.
Punters should go slow whilst they work out the
conundrum of how a man, seven years ago a compositor
in the N.S.W. Government Printing Office, is now a
reputed £30,000 bookmaker.
* * *
Darriwell, the 79 Melbourne Cup winner, has
changed hands in Queensland for £4 10s. He’ll fetch more
as cagmag when he is dead—like a deceased poet, in that.
* * *
Bungebah, the sensational Newmarket winner,
has broken a blood-vessel and been thrown out of work.
* * *
Bathurst National Advocate got ahead of all
the other N.S.W. papers in publishing news of the late
champion punching match. It stated that Slavin won.
* * *
Local authorities chant the praises, as a coming
champion, of Finnessy, of the Clarence. Another
coming champion is averred to be Lambert, of Penrith.
# # •
An attempt is being made to resuscitate Sydney
foot-racing, which died of sheer villainy a year or two
asro.
° * # *
The clever amateur rider, Mr. A. E. Wicks, had
his leg broken on Saturday in the Steeplechase, at Rand-
wick, when his mount, Singleton, ran him into the corner
of a fence. Singleton knocked one of his hips down, in-
jured himself and is now dead.
* * *
It was Dr. Cortis’ day out on Saturday, at
Randwick, when he won the Hurdles and Steeplechase
with The Duke and Harry Aulh respectively. The Duke
was recently purchased by the Doctor to replace the late
lamented Grafton.
# # *
The Coney Island (U.S.) Club has offered a
purse of £2OOO for Jackson and Goddard, provided the
latter heats M'Auliffe. This should prove another good
thing for Jackson. Goddard is over-rated. Dooley,
Choynski, and even Billy McCarthy were all able to hit
him' as they liked. Each and all of them knocked him
down, and the present writer has ever predicted that the
first hij good man Goddard meets will beat him right out
of hand. Weight is too big a factor in a Queensberry
contest to allow of men of the Dooley-Choynski type
heating a big, rushing bullocker like Goddard.
# # •
“ Tattersall ” is spoiling good business by having
big sweeps‘on races succeeding each other, Saturday after
Saturday. The sweeps on Tattersall’s and A.J.O. Winter
Stakes, run on June 4th. and 11th., only half filled and
were drawn on the preceding days, so that holders of
horses, even in Sydney, did not get their result slips till
Saturday morning, too late to do anything in the way of
hedgin *’ * * *
The children of Israel again had a good day on
Saturday at Randwick, for, barring the 3 to 1 Harry Aulh,
the odds were 10, 1(). 8 and 15 to 1.
9 * *
The doughty Costello has gone to America,
where lie will challenge all middle-weights, and may be
pitted against Fitzsimmons.
* * *
At the first English appearances of the Maori-
land team of amateur athletes, Herapton was beaten by-
Bradley, of Huddersfield, who won the 100yds by a yard,
in lOsecs. ; Batger, from scratch, ran second, in 62 l-ssecs.,
in the quarter-mile hurdles, to A. R. Williams (28yds),
whose time was filsecs. Woods fell weak in the half-mile
while holding the lead. The Maorilanders will probably
do wonders, later on.
* * *
An old friend of “ Bill ” Long’s, the gallant old
gelding Tonans, the hero of a hundred fights and winner
of a Lincoln Handicap, was destroyed a few weeks ago as
it was found that he was no longer of any use for racing
purposes. One would have thought that Tom Brown
would contrive somehow to provide a home for the old
horse in his declining days.
# * *
We are indeed a nation of sportsmen, remarks
The Bulletin’s London correspondent. The Orme cult
is now the vogue. The news of the illness of the Duke of
Westminster’s Derby favourite came like a thunderbolt
upon London, and the consternation created thereby was
almost beyond belief. Women’s Suffrage Bibs; Ulster
threatening of civil war over Home llule; even the
Anarchist outrages in France paled into insignificance
before the fact that the great Orme had a sore throat, and

better. His Grace remains at Kingsclerc in attendance
on the distinguished invalid 1” “Mostly fools.”
* *' *
There is a body called the “ Sports’ Protection
Association ” in Maoriland, with which the various athletic
clubs are affiliated. This association undertakes that a
man who is in arrears in any way to any club will net be
allowed to take part in any sport till his debts aie paid.
Recently, a leading representative footballer and athlete
was “posted,” and forthwith became bankrupt. It was
claimed for him that his bankruptcy cleared his club
subscriptions like his other debts, but the association
decided otherwise: they will not recognise bankruptcy,
and nothing hut payment in full will enable an\ man
“ posted ” to take part in any sport.

The Irishmen love steeplechasing as well as
pugilism. In the Grand National Steeplechase at Rand-
wick the other day, Nolan rode the winner, Grandwing ;
Regan rode Bonnor (2), and Keighran rode Singleton (3).

Slavin thought a couple of years’ champagning
could be taken out by a few weeks’ training.
The Carlton (Melb.) football club is called the
“ Presbyterian Twenty,” as almost every name in the
team is Scottish. The Fitzroy team, on the other hand,
although next door as a suburb to Carlton, is mostly
composed of placers whose names sound like a small
edition of the Dublin Directory. The three back men in
the latter team are Hickey, Buckley, and Monarty.
Hurroo!
HIS EXCELLENCY LORD ONSLOW
SPEAKS UP FOR
Mother Mary Joseph Auhert.
In a letter to Dr. De La Bachoue :
[Com:.]
Wellington, 18th. Septembre, 1891.
Mon Cher Docteur,—
Je vous dois de la reconnaisance pour les con-
sultations que vous m’avez donnees m’engageant
a essayer les remedes extraits des plantes de la
N.Z. prepares par la R. Mere Marie Joseph
Aubert. J’en ai fait usage pendant quelque
temps, et je puis vous affinner que je m en suit
parfaitment trouve. Ils sont toniques et forti-
fiants.
Veuillez transrnettre mes felicitations a la R.
Mere an sujet de ses travaux. Ils sont d’une
grande valeur pour le soulagement des miseres
humaines, me paraissant susceptibles d’ajouter
aux industries de la colonie, et devoir etre en
meme temps profitables aux bonne ceuvres dont
elle poursuit la realisation.
Agreez, mon cher Docteur, i’assurance de mes
sentiments distingues.
ONSLOW.
Dr. de L. Bachoue,
Consulat Francais, Wellington.
[Translation.]
Wellington,
18th. September, 1891.
My Dear Doctor,—
I feel grateful to you for your advice to try the
remedies extracted from New Zealand plants, and
prepared by the Rev. Mother Mary Joseph
Aubert. I have used them for some time, and I
am in a position to inform you that I have found
them perfectly satisfactory. They are really tonic
and fortifying.
Be good enough to convey to the Rev. Mother
my congratulations on her labours; they are of
great value for the relief of human miseries, and
appear to me capable of furthering colonial
industry, and should at the same time be profit-
able to those good works which she seeks to
realise.
Accept, my dear doctor, the assurance of my
appreciative feelings.
(Signed) ONSLOW.
Dr. de La Bachoue,
French Consulate, Wellington.
RESUSCITATION.
Everyone who has been attacked with the
prevailing epidemic of Influenza, knows that it is
very lowering to the system, and that a general
feeling of weakness permeates all through the
body.
Now, it is highly necessary that some Tonic
Medicine should be taken to bring the system up
to its proper standard, because, if not immediately
attended to, more serious complaints may develop,
and cause a large amount of unnecessary misery.
His Excellency Lord Onslow has communicated
the fact to Mother Mary Joseph Aubert that the
remedy, Karana, as prepared by herself, is, in his
opinion, and from his own experience in taking
same, a perfect tonic and very fortifying, and the
public may rest assured that the Governor of this
Colony would not give testimony unless he
was well aware that it was correct.
Those persons who are troubled with a Cough,
as an after effect from influenza, are particularly
requested to give Marcpa a fair trial, and it will
be found that benefit will result.
It may be as well to mention here that thes e
Remedies are not cheap and nasty preparations;
but are made up in such concentrated form that
they may, at first sight, appear expensive, but
this is not so, as, from the very many flattering
testimonials which have been received, the pro-
prietor is confident that value is being given, and
that only good results follow with those persons
who have given the Remedies a trial.
No medicines previously introduced into New
Zealand have reached a total sale of eighteen
thousand bottles in four months, and yet this has
been done by Mother Mary Joseph Aubert.
Kindly remember that Karana is invigorating
to the system, and that Mari; pa will successfully
combat with any Cold, Cough or Bronchial
Affection.
HE CALLED
WITH AN
ITEM
FEW things will raise Cain in a man quicker
than to feel that he has been “had.” A
person very often will make no objection to doing
a thing deliberately, but if he finds himself
unwarily let into the commission of the same act,
his whole nature rebels, and he lays himself out to
misapply all the Sunday words he has ever
learned. We had an experience like this the
other day. A bull-headed, knock-kneed, semi-
clerical looking stranger intruded his shiny
countenance around the corner of our sanctum
door, and in response to an urbane invitation to
step in, proceeded to do so, having first carefully
wiped his feet on the spot usually assigned to the
door-mat. “I called to give you an item,” he
commenced, bestowing his plug hat on one chair,
and himself on another. “ A friend of mine,” he
went on impressively, “has just died in the New
England district under very peculiar circum-
stances. He got himself mixed up with a chaff-
cutter or a steam plough, or something, and is
now—is now—well, really 1 can hardly venture
to say where he is now; but no matter. Got that
down ? Well, he was a married man with fourteen
young children. Got that down? Well, sir, this
large family is left totally unprovided for. Think
of that! without a penny piece ! No, sir, I am
not collecting for the widow. I merely wished to
draw your attention to the melancholy facts of the
case. Oh, sir, how true it is that in the midst of
life we are in death! Got that down? Perhaps
you would like to know whether the deceased was
insured ? My dear sir, that is the sad part of it—
he was not! Now, if he had been insured in a
good office such as the one I have the honor to
represent, how different! Got that down?” We
had; and while he closed his eyes to call up a
further effort, we softly rose and “ got down ” the
office club from its convenient peg, and the five
minutes on the staircase that followed were
probably the loveliest that any insurance fiend
ever experienced in a newspaper office. We are
wonderfully wiry for our years and supple of
joint, thanks to the invigorating and remedial
properties of St. Jacobs Oil. It infallibly
conquers pain, and grants a new lease of life with-
out any of its woes to the rheumatic or neuralgic
sufferers.
DOMESTIC ECONOMY.
The art of economising is one that, like all
others, may be cultivated. Economy does not
imply “pinching” or niggardliness. There is a
wide difference between true economy and the
spurious quality that demands the denial of
reasonable luxuries and the pleasures of a comfort-
able existence. It is the miser who afflcts himself,
and sins against Nature, in order that he may
hoard up wealth —most probably to be spent in
prodigality after his death. Prudence and a
judicious management of capital—the avoidance
of reckless or unnecessary expenditure—are
principal amongst the elements of true economy.
As a practical illustration let it be assumed that a
man wishes to buy a watch. What he needs to
look to is, firstly, that the watch is a good time-
keeper ; secondly, that it has a presentable appear-
ance ; and, thirdly, (the above qualities being
undeniably assured), that the price is reasonably
within his means. Now, there is but me class of
watch in the world that fits this description,
and that is the Waterbury. The Water -
burys are par excellence “ the people’s time-
keepers,” a fact that every wearer of them cheer-
fully acknowledges. They are manufactured to
suit all incomes and every class of purchaser.
Series “E,” the old long-wind favourite, a tho-
roughly reliable and serviceable watch, costs only
13s. fid. Series “J” (gent’s size), and “L”
(ladies’ size), are retailed at 225. fid., and, amongst
other improvements, are fitted with short-winding
and stem-setting appliances and enamelled dials.
More highly finished still are the latest additions
to the Waterbury family, series “K” and “N,”
for gentlemen and ladies respectively. These can
be obtained in nickel, sterling silver, or rolled
gold (with 15 years’ guarantee). In appearance,
finish, and utility they are incomparable, while
they are offered at prices that must astonish
everybody.

The Duke of Buccleuch.
An old story in new verse.
There once was a bull named the Duke of liucolcuch
Whose hide it was shiny, whose blood it was blue,
He was shipped to Australia stud-dutv lo do,
Was this highly-priced bovine, the Duke of Buccleuch.
And a lord-loving cableman sent out a line
To announce to Australia its visitor fine,
But if it was human, or if it was kine,
Was left quite in doubt—merely “Duke of Buccleuch.' 1
And Sydney lickspittledom went off its head
As the news round the club-room like lightning did
spread:
A jook, a live jook, and perhaps he ain’t wed !
Bet us hasten to welcome the Duke of Buccleuch !
When the steamer arrived, the crowd it was great,
And Circular Quay seemed to be quite eiifete ;
And the Gov. ho was there in vice-regal state
To receive his old pal the Duke of Buccleuch.
And the Mayor w'as there, too, with a speech in bis hand
To read to the Duke as he stepped on the land,
And spruce Dan O’Connor, a-smiling so bland,
All ready to cheer for the Duke of Buooleuch.
And Railway-Commissioner Eddy was there
All properly clobbered, with nicely-brushed hair,
And he had in his pocket—oh, courtesy rare—
A gold-edged free-pass for the Duke of Buccleuch.
There was horror, confusion, a frightful “ to do ”
How the larrikins laughed—and the jeers from the crew 1
And the maidens and matrons shame-facedly flew,
When they learnt ’twas a bull—the great Duke of
Buccleuch.
J. A. PHILP.
The lock-up cells at Boulia (Q ) have a
frontage to the main street. Th ed.t. incarcerants
spend their days straining themselves out of the
windows and yelling at the passers-by in raucous
tones, “For God’s sake, old man, get’s a
drink.”
The other day a Sydney man named
Hoekaday received a letter posted in England and
intended for a brother of his who resides in an
English village named Lydney. It was addressed
“Mr. Hockaday, Lydney,” but the mistake of
the post-office people in reading “L” for “S”
gave the missive an unpremeditated journey of
12,000 miles or so.
One of the features ot the Wellington
(M.L.) tug-of-war was an aged Maori lady, who
pervaded the big hall and barracked for the Irish
team. Apparently she took no interest in any of
the others, but whenever the Irishmen appeared
she cheered vociferously, and when they got hold
of the rope and gained the first inch or two, her
yells were fearful to listen to. Everybody thought
she must have broken a blood-vessel in that
stupendous effort, but, later on, when the Micks
made a terrific pull and got a foot advantage, she
gathered herself up and rent the heavens with
such howls as nobody had ever heard or dreamt of
before, and after that she screamed right on to the
end. She was a stout lady, with a tinge of red
in her hair. Possibly some wandering Pixelim
O’Toole of the past days got stranded in Taranaki
and married Hinemoa, and it was his grand-
daughter who had turned out to barrack for ould
Erin long after The O’Toole was dead and Hine-
moa was forgotten.
Judge “Water-Jug” Foster (N.S.W.)
visited the House of Commons, the other day, got
a seat in the Distinguished Strangers’ gallery, and
was par’d in the evening papers—as a grandson
of the last Speaker of the Irish Parliament.
Water-Jug, who is a red-hot Orangeman, was to
attend the great anti-Homo Rule meeting of Ulster
bigots, who, by the way, choose to ignore the
fact that every Irish rebellion, even that of ’98,
has been headed by staunch Protestants, and that
all the peaceful movements for Irish emancipation
have had Catholics at the head of them. Of
course it must have followed that in a country
where the Catholics outnumbered the Protestants
by 12 to one the rank-and-file of the rebels must
have been recruited from the larger body, but it
must not be forgotten that the Protestant party
was largely represented in the rebel ranks, too. It
is simply sickening, therefore, to hear people who
have never opened a history of Ireland solely asso-
ciating rebellion with Roman Catholicism, and
talking about handing over the “loyal minority ”
to the tender mercies of the R.C. priesthood. It
is interesting to recall, by the way, that it was the
father of Daniel O’Connell, “the Liberator,” who
informed the Irish Government of the arrival in
Bantry Bay of the French fleet which had been
invited over by Protestant rebels.
Mr. Harry Vane Milbank, who shot the
Due de Morny in a duel at Ostend a few weeks
ago, could have held his own with Ainby Bodkin,
Fighting Fitzgerald, or any other of the famous
Irish duellists of old. Milbank, who is a son of
the famous .sporting man, Sir Frederick Milbank,
whose battues are world-renowned, is a dead shot
with a pistol and he brought down the poor Duke
at the first attempt. He has already killed three
men in duels: one an attache to the Russian
Embassy in Berlin who objected to his paying tc o
much attention to his wife. Next he shot the
lady’s brother, who had the temerity to challenge
him, and then he killed the brother of his first
victim who sought satisfaction. Now that he has
winged, if not killed, a popular French Duke he
will probably be left severely alone, unless by some
one who is tired of life. Many years ago Milbank
fell desperately in love with a beautiful Australian
young lady, but her friends would not allow her
to marry him owing to his notorious liaison with
the well-known cocotte Mabel Grey, and he took
this so much to heart that he completely went to
the dogs. The late Duke of Cleveland designated
him as his principal heir, but Master Harry has
carried on such top ropes of late years on the
continent that the old gentleman altered his mind,
and left a great portion of his vast estate to Mil-
bank’s brother instead, so it is still in the family.

P. W, D'ARCY,
r HTS Company is formed with the object of establishing Refrigerating Works at the various grazing centres of New
I South Wales, for the purpose of freezing and exporting meat to the London and other markets, and there to establish
Depots with cooling chambers for its reception and disposal.
The establishment of this Company will supply a long-felt want to the pastoralists of New South Whiles, by providing a
market for their vast amount of surplus stock, which is increasing, and is of the most paramount importance to all stock owners
that there should be a market for it without any avoidable delay, so that the rapidly increasing surplus of fat stock should find
a profitable outlet through its agency.
It is to be hoped that all stock owners and others interested in the welfare of the colony will take a practical share in
establishing this Company on a sound and lasting basis, as by so doing they will reap substantial profits, directly and indirectly,
upon theirlnvestments, and establish an industry which will prove a great boon to the colony,
' No contracts of any kind have been entered into, and the whole of the future arrangements as to the erection of the
Refrigerating Works, etc., are in the hands of the shareholders. The Company will be entirely managed by the shareholders,
who will have the appointment of the Directors and all other officials.
The whole of the capital paid up will be placed to the credit of the Company, less brokerage of five per cent, on the
subscribed capital.
Shares will be allotted in order of priority of application.
Prospectuses, application forms, and all information can be obtained from the Solicitors of the Company, or from Messrs.
D’Arcy Brothers, Brokers, Scott Chambers, 96 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Gentlemen willing to be on the Provisional Board of this Company will please forward their names and addresses in
full to the Brokers.
The Railway Commissioners have written to the Brokers their willingness to supply Refrigerating Cars for the transit
of the frozen 'meat from the works to the ship’s side.
It is proposed to put works in Yass, Wagga, Hay, Cooma, Forbes or Orange, Bourke, Tamworth, Deniliquin, Dubbo or
Willino’ton, Newcastle, and such other centres as the shareholders may decide so as to embrace the whole of the colony.
To open Cooling Chambers and Australian Stores in London, there to sell the meat retail.
Lord Carrington is at present advocating in London the support of the Lords and Noblemen of England for Australian
meat and negotiations are in progress between the brokers and Mr. J. C. Rounding, representing Messrs. Westgarth & Co.,
Colonial Financiers, London, to place half the capital on the London market.
Streets, Sydney ;
Applications for shares in this Company can be made to the Australian Joint Stock Bank, Sydney, or any of its
Branches in New South Wales ; to the Company’s Solicitors, Messrs. Slattery A Heydon, Pitt and Market Stn
or to the Brokers, Messrs. D’Arcy Brothers, Scott Chambers, 96 Pitt-street, Sydney.
All Cheques made payable to the “ New South Wales Refrigerating and Meat Export Company,’’ and massed
A.J.S. Bank.
Ten thousand (10,000) shares were applied for before registration, and 15,000 are already sold.
Wanted Stock and Station Agents in every town in New South Wales to sell shares in the above Company, good
commission. Apply to Messrs. D’ARCY BROTHERS, Brokers,
Scott Chambers. 96 Pitt-street. Svdnev.

Du. Hall says : Let it be distinctly understood
that this is no scheme of quackery to draw money
out of the afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent
medicine humbug, since there is not a grain of
medicine of any kind required in the treatment.
Notwithstanding this fact it takes directly hold of
the worst cases of dyspepsia, liver complaints,
headaches, constipation, heart disease, incipient
consumption, diabetes and Bright’s disease of the
kidneys, including fevers and inflammation of the
lungs, and of other internal organs—not by
attacking these so-called diseases directly, but by
radically neutralising and removing their causes,
thus allowing nature herself to do her work unim-
peded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation, without the least danger to the most
delicate constitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to which will not be
affected favourably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.
In selling this pamphlet we are obliged to require
a “ Pledge of Honour” from those purchasing it
not to show or reveal its contents outside of their
own families. This is for our own protection, and
as the only means by which our just rights in this
discovery can be maintained. .Doctors, of course,
who purchase are not debarred from using the
treatment in their own practice, providing they do
not show tiie pamphlet or do not reveal the
rationale of the treatment.
Dr. Stevens, of Syracuse, N.Y., was the first
M.D. to whom we revealed the new treatment-
more than twenty years ago. In writing to a
friend, about a year ago, he gives the following :
... “Nearly twenty years ago, in this city,
Dr. A. Wilford Hall gave me privately a detailed
history of a hygienic treatment he had discovered
and adopted some twenty years before, and which
he had practised continuously upon himself up to
that time. The history included an account of
the failure of his health, the reasonings and con-
clusions which led him to adopt the treatment, his
rapid restoration to health, &c. From the time
of his first mention of it to me I have been
entirely satisfied that it has saved him from a
lingering consumption and death. I learn that he
has persistently continued the treatment to the
present time—forty years in all—and that his
present health and vigorous condition may right-
fully be compared to the ‘ bloom of youth,’ though
he is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in
ray practice in the twenty years past have con-
vinced me that great practical benefit will be
derived by members of the medical profession and
others from a careful study of the rationale of the
treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the details
of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely be due him
for whatever scientific advances he may be able to
unfoW in the line of assisting nature to ward off
disease, and in the restoration of health.—ll. F.
Stevens, M.D.”
Dr. Jesse Huestis, of Columbus, Ohio, referred
to in our personal statement in April Microcosm
as the second M.D. to whom the new treatment
was revealed, writes to a brother physician who
had inquired of him concerning the Pamphlet:—
“ You need have no doubt concerning the merits
of the new treatment. I consider Dr. Hall’s
statements as true in every particular. He lias in
ray opinion made the most valuable discovery of
this or any other age for the purpose of combating
diseased action in the human system, be the type
what it may. . •. . I consider the price merely
nominal when compared with the inestimable
value of the discovery. It is just as accessible to
everybody under his method of selling it, by first
requiring a pledge of honour, as would be a
doctor’s prescription, for which he might charge
the same price. In both cases the remedy is free
to all who are willing to pay for it. ... It is
without doubt the true secret of longevity', and is
as valuable to those in health as in sickness, pro-
moting sound sleep, perfect alimentation, freedom
from weariness under exertion, and a sure protec-
tion of the circulation from impurities.—J.
Huestis, M.D.”

to anyone, nor allow it to be S3en hy any person ; nor

shall be exacted not to reveal Hie treatment to others.
This pledge does not debar the physician who may pur-
