Certificate. —I have this day seen and care-
fully examined certain letters from Dr. A. Wil-
ford Hall, of New York (Hall and Co.), to John
Hern, Esq , of Melbourne.
In this correspondence it is clearly stated :
1. That John~Hern has been duly appointed
Dr. A. Wilford Hall's agent fur Australia for the
sale of the pamphlet entitled 11 Dr. A, Wilford
Hall’s Hygienic Treatment for the Cure of Disease
Without Medicine.”
2. That John Hern has been specially autho-
rised by Dr. A. Wilford Hall to give up the whole
of his time and energies to the work of selling the
aforesaid pamphlet, with a view to the alleviation
of sickness.
3. That it is Dr. Hall’s desire that no other
person whatever shall clash with the said John
Hern in any way.
4. Mr. John Hern has been fully authorised to
advertise the said pamphlet in all parts of Aus-
tralia.
o. Dr. A. Wilford Hall states to Mr. Hern that
he has other agents in Australia.
(5. That Mr. Hern is fully authorised to appoint
agents in Australia for the sale of Dr. Hall’s work.
I am convinced that the letters of appointment
are genuine, and that any person desiring to study
the mature of the hygienic discovery cannot do so
better than through the said John Hern.—
(Signed) G, G. Turbi, E.M.1.P.A., Certificated
Patent and Trade Mark Copyright Agent.

Mr. Hern, Dr. Hall's Agent.
Dear Sir. —I have been a sufferer for the last
thirty years from Bilk and Bilious Headaches,
and have tried almost everything, and obtained
no relief, all the doctors’ treatments having failed.
I began to think I should never be better. I was
advised to try Dr. Hall’s Hygienic Cure, and the
result is marvellous. Mine being a very serious
case, having suffered so long, I had to be very
careful. But now 1 feel twenty years younger
than when I commenced the treatment. I have
applied it three times a week for the first two
months, and twice a week since, and am now as
free from all signs of biliousness as though I had
never been troubled with it. It is with the
greatest gratitude I express my thankfulness for
the cure.—Yours truly, Joseph Harrison, nut
and bolt maker at Carlton Foundry, Leicester-
street, Melbourne.
Nicholson-street, Eootscray, 13th April, 1892.
Mr. J. Hern.— Dear Sir. —I am delighted to
give my testimony for what Dr. Hall’s No-Medi-
cine Treatment has done for my distressed body
during the last seven weeks. I have been a great
sufferer for the last eight years from Rheumatics
in all my nerves, Neuralgia and constant-recur-
ring serious Bilious Attacks, with most distress-
ing Headaches. During the last five years I had
lost the use of my arms, and was almost drawn
double with pain. I suffered constantly most tor-
turing agonies of pains all over my body; God
only knows my dreadful sufferings. I placed my-
self in the hands of eight doctors, and for four
months was under one of the best herbalists in
Melbourne. I had tried almost every remedy, and
taken bucketfuls of various drugs and patent
medicines. 1 took SeigeTs Syrup twice every day
for over a year, also 11 bottles of Warner s Safe
Cure, four large bottles of Clements’ Tonic, and
still I was getting seriously worse, and thought
my end very near; but for my loved ones I would
have been glad to have died. May God bless Mr.
Hobbs of this city for calling and telling me what
wonders Dr. Hall’s new cure had done for him in
a few days ! Seven weeks ago, when I procured
Dr. Hall’s Health Pamphlet from you, I was get-
ting rapidly worse, and had lost all hope of being
better. My husband and I were astonished at the
wonderful new discovery disclosed in the book. I
at once determined to give over everything else and
give it a fair trial. 1 have carefully carried out
the directions, which are easy and simple; and
now what it has done for mo is most marvellous.
I cannot find words to express all the praises it
deserves. From helplessness, seven weeks ago,
J am up at a quarter to six every morning doing
my household duties. lam really almost like a
new creature. I used to be kept awake night after
night with pain. Now my night’s rest is enjoy-
able. My biliousness, sick headache and neuralgia
have for ever gone ; the store people and neigh-
bours are amazed to see me out tripping about in
the morning and looking so well. 1 would not
give up the use of Dr. Hall’s treatment for all the
money in the world. My statement can be corro-
borated by several .storekeepers in Nieholson-street.

Station-street, Moreland, Coburg, 28th March,
1892. Mr. J. Hern. Dear Sir, —I believe that I
would render a good service to poor, suffering
humanity by giving a true statement of what Dr.
Hall’s Hygienic treatment has done for me. I
have been a great sufferer for the last ten
years from Rheumatism and a complication
of other ailments. My sufferings have been
very fearful at times from the crown of
my head to the sole of my foot. On a sudden
cold change in the weather it would cause a great
lump to rise on each side of my head, and so
severe was the pain I could only move with
greatest care. I had suffered so much and so long,
and took so much iodine and potash, and bushels
of various kinds of drugs, enough to kill a horse,
until my system was fairly poisoned throughout.
I felt and looked old and miserable at forty-two,
and I felt sure that the beginning of the end had
commenced with me. My veins were knotted,
and I told my doctor that 1 felt paralysis setting
in on me, which it proved to be. At this critical
juncture, through a neighbour’s persuasion, who
had rapidly been cimed through Dr. Hall's treat-
ment of a serious attack of Influenza, I obtained
a Dr. Hall’s Pamphlet from you, and now, after
five months' use of the wonderful “no medicine”
cure, I feel like a young man at twenty. My
constitution has been renovated and brought into
first-class working order by my blood being made
pure. Life now is enjoyable. Words cannot
express all that ought to be said in favour of this
hygienic, health-restoring process, which, I am
sure, stands unrivalled in the world. If I had it
before I got ill it would have saved me from all
my suffering and a large amount of money. lam
perfectly sure of that. I have received enough
benefit from the time I have used it to assure you
that I would not take all the money in Australia
and be without it.—Very truly yours, T. A.
Thompson.

Dr. James Rooertson, of Birmingham, England,
repeats his former endorsement as follows,
February 14: “Dear Dr. Hall,—lt will be but
justice to you and may save me answering many
private letters of inquiry if I now give you my
rather extensive 12 months’ experience of your
new treatment. As at first recognised, the general
idea is a brilliant one, physiologically sound, and
I now add —all criticism notwithstanding—so far
as I can see, it is thoroughly original. In practice,
when intelligently carried out, it meets directly
the immediate wants of a large majority of the
ailing and suffering public; it sharpens the
appetite, stimulates the liver, lightens the labour
of the kidneys, moves the bowels; by so doing it
to body and mind, chases away bilious melancholy
with its legion of neuralgia, spasms, and pains,
and comes altogether as ‘a boon and a blessing td
men.’ Personally I have benefited much by it,
and when I find a patient with sense to use if
thoroughly, I can dispense with medicine and
attend to hygiene, the true province of the doctor,
I cannot withhold my testimony in favour of what
1 know to be simple, natural, and good, a power-
ful defence to the healthy, and the best remedial
process I know of, even in cases of serious organic
disease. You may challenge the world, medical
or general, to name a process of such widely
applicable remedial power.—Faithfully yours,
James Robertson.
Rev. W. P. Hall (no relative of Dr. Hall), of
Point Peninsula, N.Y., writes; “Dr. Hal.
Dear Sir,--! have just answered a letter from
Utah Territory. A friend wished to know if my
testimonial was given correctly in the Microcosm.
1 answered yes ; that it was true to the letter, and
that my endorsement was nowhere nearly as strong
as I could now truthfully make it. 1 told him
that your treatment had cured me of rheumatism,
kidney disease, catarrh, and other complications
of disease, so that from crutches and helplessness,
I am now as well and free from disease as fifty
years ago. I have already received and answered
more than sixty letters from all parts of the
country, re-affirming the truth of my testimonial,
and I will gladly answer one hundred times sixty
if thereby I can induce sufferers to try your treat-
ment, as I declare before God my unfaltering con-
viction, both from my own experience and obser-
vation in numerous cases, that your discovery, if
properly used and persevered in, will cure any
form of curable illness that flesh is heir to. lam
ready now to proclaim to all men unreservedly
that your treatment, as set forth in your Health
Pamphlet, and without drugs of any kind, is the
healing balm for the afflicted that is capable of
supplying health to every suffering son and
daughter of Adam. Let me know how I can serve
you, and command me freely.—Your friend grate-
fully, W. P. Hall.”
ISO Rosslyn-street, West Melbourne, April 11.
1892, To Mr. J. Hern, Fitzroy. Dear Sir,—lt
gives me the greatest pleasure to give my testi-
mony to the wonderful curative power of Dr.
Wilford Hall’s hygienic treatment. During the
last seven years I have been a sufferer from indi-
gestion, and a complication of other diseases, but
during the last three years, and up to the day I
commenced to apply your hygienic treatment, my
suffering at times was very great, almost unbear-
able. Not being able to get sufficient sleep, I
became very depressed. I had consulted four
Melbourne doctors, whose remedies gave me no
lasting benefit, though I had taken a large quan-
tity of drugs and patent medicims. 1 have been
applying Dr. Hall’s new treatment a little over
two months. Indigestion is no trouble to me now ;
scarcely feel it; I can enjoy my food and sleep
soundly. I have increased 121 b. in weight. r i he
new cure has been a real God-send to me. It has
made life now enjoyable. My wife and I are
spreading the good news all we can.—Yours sin-
cerely, E. Reading.

Tales of the Early Days.
No. IX.— The Wearing of “the Shoes of
Death.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
DY PRICE WARUNO.
Convict Jones having induced (as already
related*) the boy transport, “ Kinchin ” Walker,
to steal for him from the store-tent of the timber-
gang at Birch’s Inlet, a quantity of salt, maize-
meal and other rations, felt that the least he could
do when Overseer Lemon (taking it into his head,
one day, to make a rough computation of the
stores in stock) detected the theft, was to take
upon himself the responsibility for the crime.
Not that Convict Jones did this from notions of
stript justice or because he was the lad’s tempter ;
not so. The reason was a less creditable one. He
was moved to take upon himself the Kinchin’s
punishment by the consideration that if the
Kinchin were flogged he would probably confess,
not merely to the crime, but also its object—a
“bolt.” Jones thought he himself was bound to
be flogged in any case, whether he owned up to
the theft or whether he did not, and if he
allowed the boy to be triced up, he would be run-
ning the risk of the exposure to the Overseer of
the plan for escape. And, therefore, he concluded
to take the flogging, so as to preserve from the
Overseer’s knowledge the proposal for the “ bolt ”
—and, by the same act, retain the good disposition
towards himself of the Kinchin. On this latter
point, you see, the ingenious Jones calculated
upon the Kinchin turning out to be a “ tender
morsel.”
“Now, Walker!” demanded Lemon, “there’s
a lot of stores missin’, an’ yer’ve been camp-
keeper, an’ I ’old yer ’sponsible for all th’ stuff.
Wot’s come of it ?”
As veracity does not flourish in an atmosphere of
fear, naturally Walker lied. “I—dunno, sir! - ’
he stammered, and began to whimper.
“But,” continued Lemon, “no one ever come
near the camp stores ’cept me an’ yer. Now, I
ain’t stolen them, an’ it’s on’y you fur it!”
This colloquy took place in the space between
the tents on one side of the narrow clearing
and the fern-strewn quarters—so, too courteously,
were termed the gunyahs of the convicts —on the
other. Ensconced in one of the latter, Convict
Jones heard the accusation and the denial; and
his brain worked quickly to the before-men-
tioned conclusion. He instantly took in the situa-
tion, and as speedily as he could ran out into the
clearing and confronted Lemon.
“ Th’lad speaks th’truth, O’erseer ! He don’t
know nothink ’bout it. I prigged th’ salt, an’ all
t’other stuff.”
The Overseer, surprised at this apparent mag-
nanimity, asked the self-accuser what he knew
about it, and when he stole the goods. Jones, a
brilliant liar, instantly rejoined, “Yer thort, Mr,
Lemon, ez ye’d fixed me up fur th’ night, t’other
night, by th’ gully, but yer see yer didn’t. Ez
soon ez I digged th’ grave I comes all th’ way
inter camp. Wot does I find ? W’y, th’
sodger asleep ! An’ so I gets inter th’ store-tent!
That’s wot I knows.”
“ Asleep ! the sentry asleep ! Sergeant, d’yer
’ear that?”
Sergeant Lockwood denied, the statement. It
was impossible it could have been so—asked what
was the use of believing a prisoner—swore his men
were never remiss upon guard—finally questioned
Jones,
“’Ow d’yer think ez I prigged th’stuff ?” the
scamp replied. “’E wor fast asleep a-front o’ th’
fire !”
Who was on duty that night? The Overseer
tried to find out, but the night when Jones as a
punishment was left to dig a grave and bury the
victim of a logging “ accident ” was a month back,
and no one could recollect the sentry of the time.
On outlying stations no orderly-book was kept.
“Who was the sentry, Jones?” at last de-
manded the sergeant; and Mr. Jones, having a
particular grudge against Private Stanley—
Stanley, Jones thought, was unnecessarily libei’al
of bayonet-pricks—with much show of compunc-
tion, but with considerable joy of heart, declared
that he, Stanley, was the culprit. Stanley denied
the allegation, as he could truthfully do, inasmuch
as Jones had not directly stolen the things, had
not visited the camp as he said, and, therefore,
had not seen the soldier asleep at his post. And
it was the soldier’s word against the convict’s.
The Sergeant and the Overseer confabbed.
Could they believe Jones as to the sentry’s mis-
deed ? Yes —surely! Jones would not wish to
bring punishment upon a “lobster” by a false
accusation, just when he had relieved a brother-
transport from an imputation? So they reasoned
and so they decided. They believed Jones, and
resolved to punish both that worthy and the
sentry.
At Newcastle in the early days Commandants
and other penal officers used to carry the triangles
about with them, but there was no occasion for
any like proceeding at Macquarie Harbour-suit-
able trees were convenient to every spot. Within
ten minutes of the sentence being pronounced, the
soldier was inflicting 50 lashes upon Mr. Jones,
and you can be sure that, boiling over with rage
as he was, Private Stanley could see no virtue in
mercy. In all Mr. Jones’ numerous and lengthy
experiences of flogging he was accustomed to
award the palm for severity to that inflicted by
the soldier he had falsely accused, and as the_ plan
of sentence provided that Mr. Jones should in his
turn flog Private Stanley, it was an additional
source of pain to the convict to reflect that through
his own rigorous chastisement he could not sustain
his hardly-won reputation among the Harbour
floggers of being a genuine artist with a “ cat.”
Nevertheless, Private Stanley wriggled and cried
a good deal. He could not quite see the humour
of the affair, and enjoyed no consolation from the
circumstance that his plight would have been
worse had he been the first punished. He, indeed,
seemed to feel his 25 more than Jones his 50.
Yet when the time came for the gang to “up
tools” and proceed to the logging, it was Jones
who was unable to walk—it was Jones who was
smitten with sudden weakness —and Jones who,
from languid prostration, burst out in fits of the
epileptic sort. The soldier did not —then—seem
half so affected as the transport.
Lemon was disconcerted. Even his practised
acquaintance with convict tricks (which dated
back to that trip of the coffin-ship Surprise when
the surgeon dropped some transports overboard
to see if they were dead or alive) did not warrant
him in pronouncing Jones’s attack a sham. The
froth on the mouth was there —the clenched, lip-
biting teeth—the distorted rigidity of the eye-
balls. He bent over the fellow, and sniffed at his
face. “ He’s real bad !” he then said to Sergeant
Lockwood. “ That froth ain’t soap ! ”
“ I ’udn’t trust him, Mr, Lemon,” cried the
Sergeant, “out o’ yer sight. ’E ’udn’t need th’
prayer for speedy recov’ry once yer back’s turned.
’E’ll recover wi’out prayer ! ”
It nearly always happened under the System
that the civil and military powers differed.
Authorities clashed and powers overlapped, and
the dignities of the one department were seldom
preserved without ignoring those of the other. It
was so in Sydney and Hobart Town; and the
little creatures in the outlying stations followed
the big ones of the capitals. Consequently, Ser-
geant Lockwood’s gibe at Overseer Lemon’s
judgment proved very helpful to Mr. Jones. It
confirmed Mr. Lemon in his own opinion. In a
tone he meant to be terribly cutting he responded:
“Yer ideas, sergeant, will be walued w’en they’re
axed for ! That man ain’t shammin’ Abram any
more’n Stanley, yonder, is !”
Judicious Mr. Lemon! But for that retort
Lockwood would have ordered Stanley to stop in
camp that day to get over his flogging—there was
really a regulation to that effect. Lemon’s words,
however, stung him into showing his contempt for
the Overseer’s perceptions.
“ That is just wot I ses to myself, Mr. Overseer.
’E’s shammin’ as ray man is ! Jest as much an’
no more. An’ Stanley is shammin’.” Then to the
private, who was lying on the ferns just inside
the soldiers’ tent, he spoke with all the brutality
of the little pipeclayed god :
“Stanley, ’ttention ! Turnout in guard order
at once!”
“Oh, Sergeant Lockwood ” groaned the
poor, lacerated fellow, whose first chastisement it
was.
“On guard, Private Stanley, instantly, or I’ll
place you under arrest!” And the man, helped
by a comrade, struggled to his feet. It was some-
thing, let us tell you, to be an outpost of empire
in the early days ! And
“I’m waitin’, Overseer,” grimly exclaimed
Lockwood.
* # ®
Lemon, seldom anything but suspicious,
would no doubt have waited for a few minutes to
see whether Jones’ fit passed off but for the ser-
geant. The sergeant had a right to hurry him,
and Lemon felt that to change his own view of
the transport’s illness in a moment would, of
course, have been a confession that the non-com.
was right. That he would not do. But yet to
make sure of Mr. Jones, he in the same sentence
that he bade the other gangers prepare for the
day’s work, took, as he thought, ample precaution
for that gentleman’s safety.
“Up tools, pris’ners ! An’, Walker, bring th’
slaversf an’ a pair o’ darbies out o’ my tent. I’ll
make sure o’ th’ cove, ill or well! ”
And when the boy brought the fetters and the
handcuffs, he himself put both sets of irons on the
writhing, convulsed form with a newly-scored
back.
An official who had been trained under the
ship’s surgeon who thought the surest way of
ascertaining whether a man was really dead or
only in the stupor induced by long confinement in
an un ventilated hovel, was to drop him into a
shark-infested sea, may—possibly ! —be excused
from ironing a creature he believed he had just
scourged ipto an epileptic fit. The System, ab
least, would have excused him.
» * •
Anybody but Convict Jones —when “he
came to ” would have felt hurt. But Jones was
simply philosophical.
Twenty minutes must have elapsed before he
thought it safe to open a clear and undimraed eye
upon the scene, and even then he dared not raise
his voice above a whisper in addressing Kinchin
Walker, who stood a few feet off, anxiously
watching him.
“ Oh—w’ere —am I ?” he murmured, painfully.
“ Are yer better, Mister Jones ? My, yer’ve ’ad a
bad go ! Th’ Overseer didn’t know wot to make
o’ yer !”
“Anybody ’bout ; Kinchy?” whispered the
scamp.
“No—everyone’s gone. An’Lemon ses yer was
to ’ave nothink but water all day !”
Jones then —to the Kinchin’s sudden shock —
sat up easily, without the least effort, without
pain, save a little on the back.
“ Did ’e, now? Well, that’s kind ! But there’s
two ter ’av somethink ter say ’bout that.”
“ Are yer better !”
Jones looked quizzically at the lad. “Kinchy,
my son, s’pose ez I took in Lemon, ’twar no
wonder yer war bitten. Now, yer see, sonny, ’ol
Locky war right, arter all. ”
“Lor! d’yer mean it, Mister Jones? Was yer
on’y shammin’ ?”
“ O’ course!”
“ But Lemon ses th’ froth warn’t soap !”
“ Kinchy !” He spoke with the impressive
earnestness of a professor of the moralities to a
class of clean-souled youths. “Kinchy! Th’lag
wot kint take in Lemon deserves to be a lag fur
th’ terra o’ his nateral life ! ’E war right—’twarn’t
soap, but—well, never min’ wot ’twar. ’Tis a
little idea o’ my own ! ”
“ O tell me, Mister Jones—tell me,” enthusiasti-
cally cried this promising student of gallows lore.
“ No, sonny ! Th’ knowledge ain’t for nice boys
like yer! ”
“ O do, Mister Jones —I won’t peach to no one 1
An’ it may do me a sight o’ good some time.”
“Well, by-and-bye, sonny ! Not now—time’s
too preshus. ‘ 01’ Time’s a-flyin’,’ as th’ song ses
—an’ yer an’ I’ve got much ter do ! ”
“ W’y, wot d’y mean, Mister Jones? ”
“ On’y this— that ter day's th ’ day ! ”
“ Mister Jones ! ’’ The lad clasped his hands.
“D’ye mean as we’re to bolt ter day ? ”
“ Yer’ve ’it it on th’ ’ead ! That’s wot I mean.
An’ that’s wot I meant w’en I ses as there was two
ter see ’bout my ’avin’ water an’ nothink but
water ter day. Kinchy, drag out ol’ Lemon’s
tool-chest ! ”
In the official tent was kept the camp tool-chest.
This Walker managed to drag into the open, and
Jones by sidelong motions forced himself along
the ground towards it. He looked round for a
weapon to burst the lock, but failed to see any-
thing. “Fetch er stone, Kinchy—a big’un, an’
look sharp— ‘01’ Time’s a-flyin’.’”
The lad obeyed. His own fears were swallowed
in his admiration for his precious tutor. He
brought a large stone.
“ Kin yer ’elp me up, lad ?” And with strenuous
exertion —for what with his manacles and fetters
and wounded back, Jones, notwithstanding his
mighty will, was almost powerless to help himself
—the convict stood once more erect.
“Kinchy—that box ez got ter go on its back.
Kin yer git it over ?” The boy could not turn it
over so as to bring the lock uppermost. If it had
* See Tub Bulletin, “Tales of the Early Hays: No.
VIII.—The Shoes of Death.—Tanning the Hide."
t “ Slavers’’—One (at least) of the early convict-ships
to N.S.W. had been formerly in the slave trade, and part
of her equipment was slavers’ “ bar-fetters’’-iron-bars
affixed to the ankle-rings and riveted together. Governor
Hunter ordered their disuse in Sydney ; but, in Mac-
quarie’s time, they were shipped to V.D.L., and formed
formed the first supply of fetters sent to the Harbour.

h
to be done, .Tones would have to do it himself,
And with an exertion so forceful that for several
minutes afterwards his body throbbed with great
tremblings, he did it.
“ Give me th’ stone.!” he groaned, as he brushed
from his brow the beads of sweat. He was
obliged to groan at last—and on the manacles a
drop of perspiration blistered into rust.
Kinchy Walker placed the huge piece of sand-
stone in his hands. He raised it up with such free-
dom as the impeded wrist action permitted—
swung it once or twice, the great muscles
protruding with the effort—then dashed it on the
upturned side of the chest. The wood splintered
but the tumblers of the lock held. Again was the
fragment of rook raised, again it descended ; still
again, and then the sockets gave. The lad, gasping
in admiration, could now wrench the lid open.
“ Wot’ll I give yer, Mister Jones?” he breathed,
“ Th files ! ”
The lad turned out the contents and withdrew a
rasp and a small file.
“ No,” said Jones, “give me a co’l chisel. It’ll
go through tli’ slavers quicker ’n th’
And the boy handed him one, and then the smith’s
iron-riveting hammer. The bracelets were easily
managed, and then, against one of the bazils, the
elder convict placed the edge of the chisel.
“ Hit away, Kinchy—all yer might! ”
“ But—Mister Jones, won’t it hurt badly ? ”
“Hurt. Good God! Don’t everythink hurt in
this ’ere damned ’ole ? Ain’t it all a question wot
hurts th’ littl’st ? Drive ’way, I say ! ”
The boy struck with all his force. The grim
features of the man stiffened with the pain, but
still he badetheboy go on. The only fear he had was
that the ankle-bone would be broken. Half-way
through, he told the lad to change the chisel to
the other leg. The inch-thick basil of that ankle
was also cut to half its diameter. Then he had
the Kinchin use the rasp on the uncut half, while he
himself used the smaller files on the other bazil.
In an hour he was free of the slavers.
Though with a poignancy in every pore, he,
with the assistance of the youth’s arm, raised him-
self to an erect position. Drawing himself ecsta-
tioally to his full height, he threw his hands, with
their red-ringed wrists, skywards. ■
“I’m free, now!” he shouted. “Free—free!
An’ never’ll them things, or trump’ters, or clinks,
be put on me agen wi’out some ’Stablishment
orf’cer a-bitin’ o’ th’ dust, an’ goin’ ter t’other
hell !”
At this blasphemy against the System, the boy,
affrighted, looked around. Surely so daring a
defiance would hurry Lemon and Lockwood on
the wings of retaliation? But those far from
lustreless satellites of the Powers that Were were
five miles distant: the former comfortable m the
reflection that Jones was safe m the embrace of
* *
Jones, once freed, put strenuously down
the murmurings of wounded Nature within him.
He would not admit that he was weak and full of
pain : that his back, with its weals of puffed
flesh, was an acute torture. The man’s indomi-
table will was determined not to confess, even to
himself, that there was any particular in which
the System was now his master. “Kinchy,” he
said, “yer’ll ’av ter prig some more salt fur mv
back. Rub it in well!” And the boy, wondering
but admiring, obeyed. With great spasmodic
wincings, instantly suppressed, Jones endured.
Then, with methodical haste, he ransacked the
tool-chest and the tent. Article after article was
taken up and put aside for use till some other
thing, equally useful but more portable, was dis-
covered. He bade the lad put on some of the
Overseer’s underclothing. “ Yer’ll want it, sonny,
w’en we get on th’ mountings.”
For himself, his chief concern was—boots, a
couple of tins of potted soup (a private delicacy
of the sergeant’s) a pound of tobacco—and more
boots. That is, after he had secreted two small
files and a packing-needle and twine.
“ W’y are yer so anxious about boots, Mister
Jones?” the lad asked, as Jones tied together the
two spare pairs of the two officers.
His mentor looked curiously at him: “Kin’t
yer emess, sonny ?”
“No!”
“ ’Cos, my lad, s'oosin’ —no, ’tain’t no s’posin’
at all— w’en we get ter th’ settlements, we may
get clos’, we may get hats, but we kin’t get boots
nor shoes. An’ ’tis that we’ve most reason ter be
afeard of.”
“W’y?”
“ ’Cos th’ walkin’ cuts th’ feet. Th’ perlice an’
th’ muster-masters kin alius tell a ’Arbour bolter.
Yer kin walk up an’ down th’ ’ol’col’ny fur a year,
an’ yer feet be’nt cut up like they be on th’ west
ranges, fur th’ sandstone gets inter th’ cuts, an’
th’ perlice they pick bits out, an’ it’s all up.
‘’Arbour bolter,’ they’ll say ter th’beak. ‘This
is western-stone, yer wushup ! ’ An’ they ’av yer.
Forth’soles never get better in this part o’ th’
country, w’ere 'tis tramp, tramp, tramp on ridges
or gully-stones alius. No, sonny, yer on’y ’av
yer treble fur nothink w’en yer kint land among
the settlements wi’ ’ol skin on yer feet.”
“But,” the lad whimpered, “ Wot’ll Ido? I
ain’t got no spare boots, an’ yer an’ Tooth an’
Burt ’ll take ’em if there were.”
For a moment Jones was silent. Perhaps in
that instant there was a contest between the
shreds of his better nature and the beast and devil
in him. The lad had tended him in bis fashion —
admired him —worshipped him as his protector
against the malign powers of the darkness and the
System. Would it not be better, after all, to leave
him behind? Life and hope was possible to the
youngster while at the Harbour, but if he joined
the absconders, death for him was certain. But,
if the battle did rage, the victory was not with
the good.
“ W’en yer shoes give out, Kinchy—we’ll—we’ll
carry yer ’mong us.”
There was a curious vibration in his voice, and
Kinchin mistook it for a sign of sympathy. It
was but the wretch’s sardonic appreciation of his
own double-entendre. “ Yes, we’ll carry yer,
Kinchy, lad !”
* * «
The day was wearing on to noon, when,
after a hearty meal and with the spoils of the
official stores, Jones and the Kinchin left the
camp. “Look yer last on pretty Sarah Birch,+
Kinchy ! All goin’ well, th’ Island ’ll know us no
more.” The lad, elate, grimaced coarsely towards
where in a humid haze glimmered the grey build-
ings on the sombre rock. He understood only the
surface meaning of the words.
By devious tracks, the paths of old logging
paities now largely overgrown by a species of
supple-jack, the two convicts passed from the
camp northwards for some miles, till the track
they were on ended in a water-course, which,
further on, became a tributary o: the Franklin.
Into this shallow streamlet Jones having first
hidden their stores in a ferny hollow with the
whereabouts of which on a former logging
occasion he had become acquainted, plunged,
and bade the Kinchin follow. This was done
to break the tracks. The boy would have
pulled his shoes off, but Jones told him that
an hour’s walk over the flinty pebbles would cut
his feet about so badly that when he got to the
settled country “ he’d be guve away at wuust.
’Av yer furgotten wot I tol’ yer orlready ? ” he con-
tinued, and he spoke with such a wounded irrita-
bility that Kinchin felt sorry he had been so
thoughtless as to urge his temporary inconvenience
against the instructions of his friend and teacher.
After all, it was possible for a lad to have the
vicious propensities of a man of 40, and to retain
something of the unsuspectingness of a child.
One hour’s walking on the bed of the shallow
stream, and then Jones mounted the bank again.
“We are ter cut ter th’ pits, Kinchy, ter bring
’way ail wot I’ve stored there. ’Tis ’ard work, my
boy, an’ yer’d better stay here.”
“No, no ! ” exclaimed Walker, quivering at the
bare thought of being left alone in the stifling
solitude. “Mister Jones, I’ll do anything rather
than stop here, sir. I’m so afeard o’ th’ dark.”
“ But it’s fearful work truly, sonny. It’ll take
me to cut my way ter th’ place.”
“ I’ll die, Mister Jones, to be left here,” the lad
persisted. And so it was decided that he should
go.
That passage through the forest was a foretaste
of the horrors and sufferings that were to be. Had
the lad to encounter them in company with a less
elastic or less hardy spirit than Jones, he would
assuredly have either succumbed in sheer terror,
or dragged himself back as best he could to the
camp. Now knee-deep in treacherous slime, now
traversing treeless sandstone gullies with hands
that were gashed as they clasped projecting
stones, and now breasting down the constric-
tor-like grasp of the vines, every foot’s progress
was a battle, every yard’s advance was a victory.
After three hours the early forest night
descended upon them. But while the biting of his
back increased with the frostiness of the night-air,
and his face grew haggard, Jones was drunk with
the delicious wine of freedom. And with merry
words and pleasant mimicry of Lemon and Lock-
wood’s likely manner when they reached camp
that evening, he strove valiantly to encourage his
young, almost swooning companion.
At last they halted. Through the ranged pines in
front of them was cleared a wider passage than any
they had yet met with, and Jones, peering through
the leafy veil had no hesitation in recognising the
spot. It was the place of his quest. He groped
about in the shadows, and then drew the lad to
his side. “See that mound, Kinchy?” “Yes,
sir,” he replied weariedly. But the weariness
vanished as soon as the elder told him that was
their resting-place for the night.
“We’re safer ’ere, sonny, ’n twenty mile away.
They’ll never think o’ lookin’ fur us on their back
tracks !” Then he made the lad gather ferns and
pine branches for their couches, while he got ready
their food. During the meal, with jests and sallies,
he kept the lad’s spirits up to laughter point, for
it was not his object to overtask or weary the
Kinchin at the first stage of their journey, and, as
soon as he could, he sent him to his bed of greenery,
and betook himself to his own. But, when the
lad had fallen into the deep sleep of exhaustion,
he, still borne up by his unconquerable determina-
tion, rose noiselessly and left the spot.
* e #
A hundred yards distant were the deserted
tan-pits—arelieof the first Commandant’s industrial-
experiment. During the hours of that night a
mouth back, when he said he had been engaged in
looting the store-tent, he had really been occupied
in clearing the pits of their rubbish, and in re-
filling them with grim and ghastly substances.
The body of the prisoner supposed to be buried
under the mound was in one pit swallowed in
brine, and divided into such fragments as the
scoundrel Jones considered likely to prove edible
in bis march. And in the other was his—skin.
To this last he had added, at a subsequent time,
the skin of one Bradford, a drowned convict,
whose corpse had been washed ashore. The epi-
derras were sunk in the tannic preparation. Dried
in the sunshine and exposed to the outer air, the
skin (in pieces) would prove a passable substitute
for leather.
Jones found the pits as he had left them, and
as he drew out the contents of one, and then
those of the other, he chuckled as he reflected
that he was handling the instruments with which
he was to gain his freedom. Although the hour
might well have excused even in his indurated
nature superstitious tremblings and nervous
shrinkings from his task, he thought as little of an
offended heaven as he did of the abominable
character of the actions in which he violated the
commonest instincts of human nature. For God
or Man, this genius|of the System had no tittle of
fear ; and, as for the Dark Gentleman —well, he
was Jones’ very good friend.
Through the night he toiled at the work of
removing the— food—and the—leather —to the
shelter of a piney hollow, and then he snatched
brief intervals of sleep, waking suddenly but
calmly as though the actualities of life had linked
themselves to his dream in natural sequence. In
the instant of his waking he stretched forth a
hand and covered the Kinchin’s mouth, shaking
him the while. “Kinohy, hark!” he whispered.
“ Kinchy!”
There was no carol of birds to hail the Mac-
quarie Harbour day ; but there were sounds
approaching that stirred the stillness not un-
musically. They were the beat of tramping feet
and the jingle of soldiers’ accoutrements.
“ Oh, Mister Jones !” the lad gasped.
“Hush, yer fool ! Don’t yer see they’re not
a-huntin’ us ? They’re goin’ ter work ter day.”
“ But they’re cornin’ here !”
“No, they pass us by. Be quiet ez a stiff,
now !”
The feet drew near, the regularity of the tramp
slipped into a rustle and scrape as the gully was
descended and ascended, there wrs a drawing of
deep breaths as the gang rested for a second before
resuming their tramp, and then a succession of
short, sharp coughs.
Kinchy, his eyes fixed with terror on Jones’
face, saw to his joy, a half-smile disturb its grim
hardness. The lad would have spoken, but dared
not whisper. Only five yards’ thickness of scrub,
which separated them from the gang, and even a
whisper might have carried. And not till long
after silence regrasped the spot did even Jones
murmur a word.
“ Did yer ’ear ’em cough, Kinchy ? ”
“ I heard three or four coughing.”
“ That war Bunt an’ Toothy, sonny. They saw
it, an’ll be here ter night.”
“ Wot is it, Mister Jones, an’ wot d’you mean ?
How can they be ’ere ? ”
“ I broke a saplin’ top, yesternight, Kinchy,
an’ Bunt an’ Tooth saw it, an’ coughed back.
They knew I war ’bout.”
“ O, Mister Jones —mightn’t they split? ”
“ Split—split! Me an’ them ’av sworn the oath
i —yer too young yet, Kinchy, ter take it, but yer
} Sarah Island, named after Mrs. Sarah Birch, of Hobart

the lady's name.

know th’ oath is death ter them as take, an’ split.
An’ ’sides, I kin get ’em ’way from the ’arbor if
anyone kin. An’ wi’out me, they’re lost. An
now let’s ’av breakfast, sonny.”
“ Are we safe, so close?”
“Safe!” The rascal laughed. “Oh, Lemon
’ll work everybody double ter-day, he’ll be so mad
after losin’ o’ me an’ yer. We’re safer ’n if he’d
been huntin’.”
The lad set about mixing oatmeal and water —
this, sugar-sweetened, was to be his savoury meal.
He stirred the mess fora minute, and then, gazing
trustfully into the elder man’s face, said, “I do
wish, Mister Jones, as we was goin’ by ourselves.
I don’t like Bunt nor Tooth.”
Jones was cutting off a piece of tobacco, which
precious luxury was to be his breakfast —“ baccy ”
was necessary and luxury and all to such a
wretch ; but, though his mouth was watering for
the only morsel for which he then craved, he
paused to soothe the young fellow’s mind.
“Now, Kinchy, don’t yer cherish prejudices.
Prejudices ez bad. They’re not bad coves, ain’t
Toothy an’ Bunt, an’, look ’ere, Kinchy ” ho
paused. The boy waited eagerly, there was such
a tone of sympathetic confidence in the rascal’s
voice.
“Look ’ere—l mean for Bunt an’ Toothy ter
carry yer, lad, w’en yer boots get done. I’ll ’av
miff ter do ter carry— th’—th’ grub, yer see.”
“Oh, Mister Jones —yer are kin’ ter think o’
me like that.” The lad could have kissed his
mentor in his gratitude.
How could he know that Mr. Jones was men-
tally cursing himself for his carelessness ? He had
only stopped himself just in time from saying
“ the other grub.” And that remark might have
put even so simple-minded a lump as the destined
“tender morsel” on his guard. It was a narrow
escape, Jones thought, for himself, and Bunt and
Tooth. Food would be short enough as it was.
# * *
Food was short a month later. And the
promised land of the settlements was still a month
(if they were lucky) or two (if they were not)
ahead.
They were encamped in a gully of what is now
known as the King William Range. Between
them and the settled country lay granite and
sandstone mountains, quagmirish morasses, forests
as yet untrodden by foot of man. The native
tiger and the devil were the tenants of this
region. They thrived because, as yet, man was
not. Only the convict.
Bunt and Tooth were of the party. By one of
those pieces of strategy of which the convict mind
was so fertile, they had, the day following the
flight of Jones and the boy, induced Overseer
Lemon to send them to a spot a few score yards
distant from the main body of loggers.
A soldier went with them, the unfortunate
Stanley. One pine was felled, and then they
moved a little further away, and half-cut through
another trunk. Then the whistle recalled them to
the mid-day rest of haif-an-hour's space. _
The meal ended, they returned to their work,
their movements being aided by the point of
Stanley’s bayonet. Swinging their axes vigor-
ously, they had momentarily disarmed the
soldier’s suspicions, and he had come carelessly, in
the path of Ins “ walk,” within the reach of their
tools. A flash of thought passed from eye to eye,
the transports wheeled simultaneously, and blade
met blade in the sentry’s head. He went, with-
out a groan, to report himself in another world—a
paltry unit of some dozens of poor devils who
similarly died on guard. The three-and-a-ha f
hour’s lapse before nightfall enabled the murderers
to make a circuit towards the gully. Nor were
they pursued, for Lemon, with his weakened staff,
dare not send out searchers. After all, it was
only a “codger” that was left uncoffined ; and his
experience was not exceptional. One Overseer
Francis had taken a logging-gang to_ the Gordon
River, of six soldiers and 14 convicts. ihree
soldiers and two transports returned.
Now Bunt and Tooth, pleased to beof the party,
had been as solicitous of Kinchin Walker’s welfare
in the early stages of the march as Jones himself.
But when they had left the Deception Ranges
behind them and were penetrating what we
now know as Calders Pass in the hope of
striking the flat, marshy country between the
Great Lake and Lake Echo through which their
path lav towards the midland settlements, they
grew petulant with him. The purloined stores
had long been exhausted, and the fast diminishing
stock of other “ food ” suggested anxiety as to the
future. The larder was tolerably well preserved,
but when demands upon it would cease in the one
and same instant that they replenished the supply,
why defer the replenishment ?
So, one morning, when the Kinchy had been
sent out of hearing, they, very respectfully, as
befitted the fine qualities of Jones’ leadership,
petitioned him to bring matters to a, conclusion,
if not for them, for the Kinchin. They insisted
upon it being put to the lot.
Jones demurred, urged they should wait a little
longer, accepted their refusal with perfect
equanimity, and consented to put the question to
the lot. With the pleasant sense of humour
which was one of Ids characteristics, he then
suggested making the lad the judge in Ids own
cause. .
“Kinchy,” he said, quietly. “Toothy an me
’s ’ad a dispute. Pick up some leetle stones, will
yer?” The lad obeyed.
“Now, it’s odd or evens, Toothy. If th’
Kinchy ’olds out odd as yerses right, then you’ve
yer way. If t’other, I’ve mine.”
“ Wot’s it about, Mister Jones? ” asked the lad.
“ Mayn’t I know ? ”
“ Well yes, sonny. Yer see them boots o’
yourn’s gettin’ bad in the sole, an’ tis ter decide
w’ether we carry yer or not.”
The lad, gratified by this fresh proof of Jones’
care for him—for he did not doubt that Jones was
desirous he should be carried—smiled, put his
hands behind his back, fumbled with the pebbles,
thrust forth his closed right-fist.
“Wot is it, Mister Tooth ?” he questioned.
“ Odds !” cried Tooth. And odds it was. The
lad’s fingers opened and disclosed the three
stones.
“Yer turn, Jones!” said Tooth, “ter see as
who carries the lad.”
Again the doomed boy, innocent still of any
suspicion, played behind his back with the
pebbles.
“Evuns!” exclaimed the arch scoundrel.
“ Evuns !” But again it was odds ! One stone
wai seen on the outstretched palm.
“ Yer’ve ter do it!” shouted Bunt and Tooth
together.
And Jones nonchalantly nodded.
* * *
He passed towards where their bundles
lay, stooped over them for a few minutes, ex-
tracted something, and returned towards the
group. ,
“Kinchy,” he said, with never a thrill in a
syllable, “yer’ve ter be carried. Get ready !”
“How, 'Mister Jones? Wot d’yer mean by
gettin’ ready?” The lad, still unsuspectingly,
looked fearlessly into the murderer’s face.
“ Yer’vo to be carried dead, Kinchy !’
“O, God ! D’yer mean it, Mister Jones ? Ihe
lad was livid and choking ; the sudden revelation
of his fate, now too clear, dammed back the courses
of his blood and his speech,
“ D’ I mean it!” The whole brute came up
now in Jones, “ Yer alius axing that, as ef yer
doubted my word. Ido mean it!”
“ God ! An’ I—helped yer—to get free, Mister
Jones!” , ,
“ Get ready. If yer ’av any knowledge o- God,
an’ as yer called on Him p’r’aps yer ’av, say yer
prayers!” I r
The lad looked at Tooth and Bunt. He saw
nothing but implacable doom on the latter s
features. There was a twitching of Tooth s bps.
It may have been the symptom of sympathy
for the victim ; or, again, it may have been the
corning joy that had touched them into softness.
But the lad saw only hardness. And knelt.
No clergyman to this time had visited the Har-
bour, The System, however, was a handmaid to
religion. She gave her children prayers week y
with the Regulations. But the lad could only
remember the “ Grace before Meat,' because of
its more frequent repetition.
He tried in his terror to pray.
“ Our Father ’’—prompted Bunt.
“ p ol — wo t we’re ’bout to receive . His head
fell forward before he could whisper the conclusion
which would have been so terribly ironic in that
fatal moment. A swoon, merciful to the lad,,
made Jones’ fell deed the easier.
Yet, in all truth, the lad should have, been
thankful for that knife blow. Better death than
the System.
* * *
When the Three passed through the marsh
country they carried with them a greatly lightened
burden. Where others had died from famine
they had lived. And their feet were covered
—how? Literally, and in a double sense, in their
march they were” sustained by the flesh of the
dead They walked in mocassins of human skin
—in veritable shoes of death. If ever Macquarie
Harbour “ bolters ” had a chance of escaping the
identification as such which usually followed upon
an examination of the soles of their feet, it was
the Three. Inside the fragments of their ration
boots, they wore shoes of their own manufacture.
Liberty never knew her own grandeur till she
understood how she was adored by the Harbour
felons.

You’ll Triumph Not in This Land.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
You who speculate in Labour—whether land or
mines your spoil—
You who gamble with the harvests of the children
of the soil!
We impeach you as embezzlers —we —the Southern
sons of toil.
In the peace of long possession you are blind and
you are bold,
You have fortified tire mountains, and your guns
are manifold ;
But you’ll triumph not in this land as you
triumphed in the old !
Go and boast of “our resources,” but the land
shall not progress
In the poverty of labour and the wealth of idle-
ness !
Even tho’ the nations pass us while we struggle
for redress.
Shear us while you can in comfort—not for long
you’ll rob and rest,
There are men of settled purpose in the regions of
the West,
Hearts of gold to be discovered when the proper
spring is press’d.
In the dim and hazy regions that are opposite the
morn
There’s the spirit of a nation ! —there’s the pat-
riotism, born
In the darkness of your blindness and the silence
of your scorn.
And the land-shark will not stop it with the
wealth at his command,
Nor his everlasting fences stretching out across
the land
(Driving farmers and selectors to the barren soil,
and sand).
You have conquered for a season, we are bought
and we are sold,
You have strength as yet to rule us, and your
selfish hearts are cold,
But you’ll triumph not in this land as you
triumph’d in the old.
Sydney, May, ’92.
HENRY LAWSON.
In Memoriam.
AN AUSTRALIAN POET.
Alas, that he whose vision hold so much—
Who pierced to where those dim-hid mysteries,
Locked in the heart of wild-wood shrubberies,
Throb out their secrets in low melodies
Ilis fine ear caught—should rob us, by that touch
Of hasty eagerness to grasp it all,
And leave us yearning vainly o’er his pall !
What chords untouched lie deadened in the clay !
Each unvoiced creek and gully mourns his loss ;
The tall trees bend their mourning heads across
With sighing whispers to the winds, who toss
The withered leaves in saddest disarray,
Till God send down a like so gifted tongue—
Till then—a silence—barren, void, unsung.
J.H.G.

The secret of keeping young at the age of
seventy: Never do anything yourself which you
can get another to do for you ; never trouble your-
self as to who will get credit for what is done;
never work after three o’clock in the afternoon ;
sleep 10 hours out of every 24.—Dr. E. E. Hale.
We have received from Messrs. Harrold
Bros, a sample of Hiram Walker and Sons’
(Walkerville, Ontario, Canada) Canadian Club
whisky—a mellow and pleasant-flavoured spirit of
the Bourbon order. In Canada there is a most
stringent law in force, preventing any spirit from
being sold until it has arrived at the age of two
years, and to ensure this the Government retains
it in its possession for that length of time after
manufacture—a precaution that is not taken by
any other Government in the world. Not only
has the consumer of whisky in Canada the
advantage of an enforced minimum age; he has
also this security—the benefit of which is shared
by the producer—that every bottle of whisky has
its age guaranteed by Government. Which, now
that Victorian brandy promises to gain a place at
the top of the European market, affords a valuable
hint to .the Australian Governments. The
enormous success which has attended the export-
ing of butter for Victoria under official inspection
should lead to further experiments in a similar
direction.

“There is a strong strain of brutality in
the Arab and his descendants,” remarks Sydney
Echo. “ The wars of that fierce race show it
over and over again.” So they do. These Arab
wars are not pleasant reading, and when we feel
sick of carnage we like to turn from them to the
refreshing page of the daily paper, and learn
about the man whose ear was chewed off, and the
lady who walloped her child to death, and the
baby-farmer who starved infants in a cellar, and
other glorious evidences of the advance of civilisa-
tion. The Arabs are a disgusting crowd.
“ Fewer Peighs ” is the mysterious entry,
still preserved, of a levy made by the Eureka
insurgents at a Ballarat pastrycook’s.
With Boldrewood’s “ Nevermore,” it is as
with Scott’s “ Antiquary ” : The critics decried it
at first, but perceive now it is one of his best, if
not his best.

line, but after the banquet the champagne
accomplished what an interviewer couldn’t:
Playford went off spontaneously. Griffith and
the “black push” are now seriously considering
teetotalism. —B.
My Poem.
I wrote a little ballad of the Bush,
A simple, touching tale I thought would please,
I brought in “mallee scrub” and the “soft rush
Of wind through silver gums and wattle-trees.”
I had a “swagman with his bluey,” too,
A “gunyah,” and “ a cool tobacco cloud,”
Some “dingoes ” and “ an old-man kangaroo,”
A “stockman”and a “squatter bad and proud,”
A “ dark lagoon ” still haunted by the dead,
A “dried-up creek,” and then “a nameless
grave,”
With “station children romping overhead.”
I took it to the editor and gave
It to him that he might peruse my lay,
And waited, confident of praise and pay ;
He read and raised his foot —no more I’ll say,
But that is why I don’t sit down to-day.
J.M.A.
